Divine Decree

Al-Husayniyya vs. Mu'tazila: Who Really Denies Human Power?
Maturidi compares al-Husayniyya and al-Mu'tazila on human power and jabr accusation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Mu'tazila's accusation that the Husayniyya are Mujbira, and see why he argues the Mu'tazila are more deserving of that label.

Core Idea
The Husayniyya grant more power to humans than the Mu'tazila, so calling them compulsionists is unfair.

Maturidi presents a fascinating debate: which theological school truly denies human power? The Mu'tazila accuse the Husayniyya of jabr, but Maturidi turns the tables. Let's examine his argument and see who ends up looking more like a compulsionist.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Accusation
Read the opening line where the Mu'tazila label the Husayniyya as 'Mujbira'. Notice the reason given: the Husayniyya say the servant has power only for the action he is doing, not for its opposite.
This sets up the debate.
2
Compare the Two Positions on Power
Read the middle section where Maturidi explains what each school affirms: the Husayniyya affirm power and choice for the present action; the Mu'tazila deny all power at the time of action.
Pay attention to the claim that the Husayniyya actually give more power.
3
Analyze Maturidi's Rhetorical Conclusion
Read the final question: which description is more similar to jabr? Reflect on Maturidi's implicit answer and why he accuses the Mu'tazila of lack of modesty.
This is the key insight of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سمّت المعتزلة الحسينية مجبرة بما قالت الحسينية للعبد قدرة ما هو فيه من الفعل، وليست له قدرة ضده وقت الفعل وقبل ذلك الوقت. [ف]الاختلاف بينهم وبين المعتزلة إنما هو في الاسم خاصة، لأن الحسينية تقول: «هو [قادر] على ماهو فيه، فعند الله لطف لم يعطه»، والمعتزلة يقولون: «لم يبق عند الله شيء فيه صلاحه إلا وقد أعطي». فقد اتفقا على قدر ما أعطاه؛ ولا قوة له وقت الفعل عند المعتزلة، وعند الحسينية له قدرة ما هو فيه، وله اختيار ما هو فيه. فكان الذي معه من القدرة والاختيار أكثر من الذي عند المعتزلة. فكيف سمتهم المعتزلة مجبرة لولا قلة الحياء ؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله . والأصل عند الحسين أنه عند الفعل مضيّع أحد القدرتين ولا عذر له في التضييع ؛ وعند / المعتزلة لا قدرة له لا بالتضييع ولا غيره. فأي الوصفين أشبه بالجبر لو كان ثمة إنصاف ؟
Then the Mu'tazila named the Husayniyya 'Mujbira' (compulsionists) because the Husayniyya said that the servant has power for the action he is engaged in, and he has no power for its opposite at the time of the action and before that time. So the difference between them and the Mu'tazila is only in the name, because the Husayniyya say: 'He is capable of what he is doing, but God has a grace (lutf) that He did not give him.' And the Mu'tazila say: 'There is nothing left with God that contains his welfare except that He has given it.' So they agreed on the extent of what He gave; and he has no power at the time of the action according to the Mu'tazila, while according to the Husayniyya he has power for what he is doing and has choice for what he is doing. Thus what he has of power and choice is more than what the Mu'tazila have. So how could the Mu'tazila name them Mujbira, were it not for lack of modesty? And there is no power except with Allah. And the principle with al-Husayn is that at the time of action, the servant is one who wastes one of the two powers and has no excuse for the wastage; while according to the Mu'tazila he has no power at all, neither by wastage nor otherwise. So which of the two descriptions is more similar to jabr, if there were fairness?
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage presents a comparison between the Husayniyya (followers of Husayn al-Najjar) and the Mu'tazila regarding human power and the charge of jabr. Maturidi begins by stating that the Mu'tazila labeled the Husayniyya as 'Mujbira' because the Husayniyya affirm that a person has power only for the action he is doing and not for its opposite at that time. He argues that the difference is merely verbal, as both groups agree that God provides a certain capacity. The Husayniyya, however, maintain that the servant has power and choice for the action he performs, while the Mu'tazila deny any power at the time of action. Thus the Husayniyya actually grant more power to the servant. Maturidi then asks how the Mu'tazila can call them compulsionists, implying that the Mu'tazila are less modest. He further contrasts the views on the loss (tadyi') of power: the Husayniyya say the servant wastes one of two powers, while the Mu'tazila say he has no power at all. The passage ends with a rhetorical question: which description is more similar to jabr?

Key Terms
حسينية (Husayniyya)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)
قدرة (qudra)
جبر (jabr)
لطف (lutf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that affirming a single power for the servant (only for the current act) is closer to jabr because it limits ability.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazila's complete denial of any power at the time of action is even more restrictive, making them more akin to compulsionists.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the key difference between the Husayniyya and the Mu'tazila regarding power at the time of action?
A) The Husayniyya deny all power, while the Mu'tazila affirm power for the present act.
B) The Husayniyya affirm power for the present act, while the Mu'tazila deny all power at the time of action.
C) Both affirm power for the present act, but differ on the opposite act.
D) Neither affirms any power at the time of action.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila, by denying any power to the servant at the time of action, are more deserving of the label 'Mujbira' than the Husayniyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult choice, do you truly believe you have power over your actions, or do you act as if your choices are forced? How does Maturidi's contrast between the Husayniyya and Mu'tazila challenge you to acknowledge your own responsibility before Allah without falling into despair or arrogance?
?
The Mu'tazila claim that God has given everything needed for your welfare. But Maturidi hints that God withholds some grace (lutf). Are you willing to accept that divine wisdom may hold back blessings, or do you demand full power and knowledge from yourself? Reflect on a time you felt you lacked the ability to do good. How does your understanding of qadar affect your reliance on Allah?
?
Maturidi points out that the Husayniyya, despite limited power, still hold the servant accountable for wasting one of his two powers. Can you think of an area in your life where you 'waste' the power Allah has given you—such as time, ability, or intention—while excusing yourself because you lack the opposite? What step will you take today to stop making excuses and use what Allah has provided?

The Mu'tazila as the True Determinists
Maturidi provides a proof that the Mu'tazila are the true Jabriyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Mu'tazila, despite affirming human will, effectively endorse jabr by denying its efficacy.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's own words show they are the true Jabriyya.

Maturidi presents a surprising argument: the Mu'tazila, often seen as champions of free will, are in fact the true determinists. He uses their own statement to show that they strip human will of any real efficacy, leading to jabr. This passage challenges common assumptions and forces us to think about what genuine human agency requires.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi begins by quoting the Mu'tazila's own claim about human action.
The quote is central to the argument.
2
Identify the key Mu'tazili statement
Find the phrase: 'The action belongs to the servant whether he wills or refuses.' Why does Maturidi consider this a form of jabr?
3
Understand Maturidi's reasoning
Maturidi argues that removing will from action makes the person necessarily evil, ignorant, or incapable. Then he points out that the Mu'tazila attribute to the servant a will that contradicts God's will, which is a sign of coercion.
4
Contrast with the true Jabriyya
Note how Maturidi distinguishes the 'true Jabriyya' who criticized compulsion because they recognized God's sovereignty, and then accuses the Mu'tazila of speaking foolishly about God.
The phrase 'There is no power except with Allah' ends the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي يحقق أن المعتزلة هي المجبرة قولهم : للعبد الفعل شاء العبد أو أبى. ومن زالت عنه المشيئة في فعل فهو ساءٍ أو جاهل أو عاجز لا يخلو عن ذلك. مع ما قد جعلوا للعبد أن يريد في سلطان الله ما لا يريده ويشاء في ملكه ما لا يشاؤه، وهو يشاء خلافه ويريد غيره، وذلك علامة القسر والجبر. فعابت الجبرية في جبر العبد بما رأوا الله الملك والجلال، ثم قالت رب العالمين سفها بغير علم ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then what confirms that the Mu'tazila are the Jabriyya is their statement: 'The action belongs to the servant whether he wills or refuses.' And whoever has will removed from him in an action is either evil, ignorant, or incapable, and cannot be free from that. Along with what they have established for the servant: that he wills in the dominion of God what God does not will, and wishes in His kingdom what God does not wish, while God wills the contrary and wants something else. That is a sign of coercion (qasr) and compulsion (jabr). For [the true] Jabriyya criticized the compulsion of the servant because they saw [in God] sovereignty and majesty, and then they (i.e., the Mu'tazila) said to the Lord of the worlds foolishly without knowledge. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage provides a proof that the Mu'tazila are actually the true proponents of jabr (compulsion). Maturidi begins by citing the Mu'tazila's own statement: 'The action belongs to the servant whether he wills or refuses.' This effectively removes human will from the equation, which is a key feature of jabr. He then argues that if a person has no will in an action, he is necessarily either evil, ignorant, or incapable. Furthermore, the Mu'tazila attribute to the servant the ability to will and wish something contrary to God's will within God's dominion, which Maturidi considers a sign of coercion (qasr) and compulsion (jabr). He contrasts this with the true Jabriyya, who criticized the compulsion of the servant because they recognized God's sovereignty and majesty. The Mu'tazila, in their response to the Jabriyya, are accused of speaking foolishly about God. The passage ends with the phrase 'There is no power except with Allah.'

Key Terms
مجبرة (Mujbira)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)
جبر (jabr)
قسر (qasr)
مشيئة (mashi'a)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila are the opposite of Jabriyya because they affirm free will.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazila's position actually leads to jabr by removing the efficacy of human will.

Checkpoint
What is the proof that al-Mu'tazila are the true Jabriyya according to Maturidi?
A) They claim God creates all actions
B) They say the servant's action occurs whether he wills or refuses
C) They deny all human power
D) They accuse others of jabr

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the Mu'tazila's affirmation of human will without efficacy reduces to determinism, making them the true Jabriyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila said: 'The action belongs to the servant whether he wills or refuses.' In what situation in your life do you act as if your choices do not matter because you feel compelled by circumstances? How does this expose a gap between what you claim about your will and what you actually believe about God's control?
?
Maturidi reveals that claiming to will something without it affecting the outcome is a form of determinism. Where do you say you intend to do something for Allah, but deep down you have no real resolve to act? What would it mean for you to truly will with sincerity, knowing that all power is from Allah?
?
The passage ends: 'There is no power except with Allah.' If you lived this truth fully today, what would change about how you approach a specific task or challenge you have been avoiding? What one step will you take to depend on Allah's power rather than your own?

Agreement on the Meaning of Power
Maturidi presents the agreement between al-Husayn and Mu'tazila on the disbeliever's state despite verbal differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila agree on the reality of the disbeliever's lack of power for faith, but differ in naming it as tawfiq and 'isma versus khidhlan.

Core Idea
Agreement on essence, dispute on naming: the real issue is what constitutes strength for faith.

Maturidi begins by examining a case where the Mu'tazila criticize al-Husayn's wording but agree with his underlying meaning. The example of the disbeliever's power illustrates that the real theological dispute is not about the fact but about the terminology. This lesson explores that agreement and clarifies the true point of contention.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the structure of the argument.
Notice the parallel statements from both sides.
2
Identify the agreement
Both sides agree the disbeliever lacks tawfiq and 'isma; one calls it 'no power for faith', the other 'khidhlan'.
The underlying state is identical.
3
Understand the real issue
The real issue is about making tawfiq and 'isma the strength of faith, and khidhlan the strength of disbelief.
This is the substance, not the terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر طرفًا مما عابت المعتزلة حسينا في النطق ووافقته في التحصيل. قال الحسين: الكافر وقت كفره ليست له قدرة الإيمان؛ وقدرة الإيمان عنده التوفيق والعصمة ووافقته المعتزلة على أنه ليس بمعصوم ولا موفق، بل هو مخذول متروك على رأيه، وذلك معنى قدرة الكفر عند الحسين. فاتفقا على المعنى الذي اختلفا في اسمه فحق المسألة بينهم في جعل التوفيق والعصمة قوة الإيمان والترك والخذلان قوة الكفر، لا [في] إفراد التكلم في القدرة والإغضاء عن حقيقة ما يجب القول به. وبالله التوفيق. وقال الحسين: معنى الإرادة في الله سبحانه أنه لم يغلب ولم يُقهر. وقد أعطت المعتزلة هذا المعنى في جميع أفعال الخلق: إنه لم يُغلب ولم يُقهر، فتبطل المسألة في الإرادة؛ إنما بقيت في تأويل الإرادة لا غير. مع ما كان من قول الحسين: «إن أفعال العباد مخلوقة، فأراد خالقها كونها على ما خلقها». ومذهب المعتزلة أنها ليست بمخلوقة لله، فتكون المسألة في خلق الأفعال لا في الإرادة. وقال الكعبي: الإرادة معناها أنه مختار غير مغلوب، فمثله في كل شيء يلزمه. ثم المعتزلة ليست تُثبت الله إلى العالم سوى أن كان ولم يكن عالَم، ثم كان عالم، فصار بذلك المعنى خالقا له، مريداً على الوجه الذي ذُكر. فقال الحسين في أفعال العباد: إنه إذ كان ولا هذه الأفعال، ثم كانت هذه، وكانت بإرادته التي تأويلها ما وصفه، وكان خلقها بأن كان ولم تكن هي. ولا قوة إلا بالله. على أن "الحسين" يجعله في الأول مريداً لكون الخلق على ما كان، وكذلك لكون كل مخلوق على ما كان بإرادته. والمعتزلة تنفي معنى الإرادة، لا تجعل غير أن كان ولم يكن الخلق ثم كان؛ فحق ذلك فيه أوجب. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقالت المعتزلة: الوعيد يأخذ من أخرجه فعله عن الإيمان، وكذلك قال الحسين وجميع أهل الإرجاء: أن كان من استحق بفعله زوال اسم الإيمان فهو كله في النار أبدا. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والاختلاف بين هؤلاء فيما به يخرج من الإيمان لا في حق الوعيد، فالاحتجاج بآي الوعيد في المسألة خطأ.
Then we mention a portion of what the Mu'tazila criticized al-Husayn for in wording but agreed with him in outcome. Al-Husayn said: 'The disbeliever at the time of his disbelief has no power for faith; and the power for faith with him is tawfiq (divine enablement) and 'isma (protection).' And the Mu'tazila agreed with him that he is not protected (ma'sum) nor enabled (muwaffaq), but rather he is forsaken (makhdhul) and left to his opinion, and that is the meaning of power for disbelief according to al-Husayn. So they agreed on the meaning on which they differed in name, so the truth of the issue between them lies in making tawfiq and 'isma the strength of faith, and abandonment (tark) and forsaking (khidhlan) the strength of disbelief, not in speaking separately about power and ignoring the reality of what must be said. And with Allah is success. And al-Husayn said: 'The meaning of irada (will) in God, exalted is He, is that He has not been overcome nor coerced.' And the Mu'tazila granted this meaning in all actions of creation: that He has not been overcome nor coerced, so the discussion about irada becomes invalid; it remains only in the interpretation of irada, nothing else. Along with what al-Husayn said: 'Indeed, the actions of servants are created, so their Creator willed their existence as He created them.' And the doctrine of the Mu'tazila is that they are not created by God, so the issue becomes about the creation of actions, not about irada. And al-Ka'bi said: 'Irada means that He is a chooser (mukhtar) not overcome (ghalib), so similarly in everything that follows for Him.' Then the Mu'tazila do not affirm [any relation] of God to the world except that He was and there was no world, then the world came to be, so by that meaning He is its Creator, willing in the manner mentioned. So al-Husayn said concerning the actions of servants: 'He was and these actions were not, then they came to be, and they came to be by His irada whose interpretation is what he described, and His creation of them is that He was and they were not.' And there is no power except with Allah. Moreover, al-Husayn makes Him in the beginning willing that creation be as it was, and likewise that every creature be as it was by His irada. And the Mu'tazila deny the meaning of irada, they do not posit anything except that He was and creation was not, then it came to be; so that makes the truth more binding in it. And there is no power except with Allah. And the Mu'tazila said: 'The threat (wa'id) applies to the one whose action removes him from faith.' And likewise al-Husayn and all the Murji'a said: 'Whoever by his action deserves the removal of the name of faith is entirely in the Fire forever.' And there is no power except with Allah. And the difference between these groups is concerning that by which one leaves faith, not concerning the right of the threat, so using verses of threat as evidence in the issue is wrong.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed analysis of the areas where al-Husayn (founder of al-Husayniyya) and the Mu'tazila agree in essence but differ in wording. Maturidi begins by citing the example of the disbeliever: al-Husayn says the disbeliever has no power for faith, and defines power for faith as tawfiq and 'isma. The Mu'tazila agree that the disbeliever is not ma'sum or muwaffaq, but rather makhdhul and left to his opinion. They agree on the meaning but differ on naming. The real issue is about what constitutes strength for faith or disbelief. Next, Maturidi discusses the meaning of irada (will) in God. Al-Husayn defines it as God not being overcome or coerced. The Mu'tazila accept this meaning for all actions of creation, so the dispute reduces to the interpretation of irada, not its reality. Al-Husayn further states that actions of servants are created and God willed them. The Mu'tazila deny that actions are created by God, shifting the dispute to the creation of actions rather than irada. Al-Ka'bi's definition of irada as 'chooser not overcome' is also noted. Maturidi then contrasts the views: the Mu'tazila only affirm God's existence before the world and His becoming Creator when the world comes to be, while al-Husayn extends divine will to the moment of creation. Regarding the divine threat (wa'id), both al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila agree that those who lose the name of faith by their actions will be eternally in Hell. However, their disagreement is about the specific actions that remove one from faith, not about the threat itself. Citing verses of threat is thus an error in this context.

Key Terms
تحصيل (tahsil)
توفيق (tawfiq)
عصمة ('isma)
خذلان (khidhlan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on what do al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila agree regarding the disbeliever?
A) He has power for faith
B) He is not protected nor enabled
C) He has power for disbelief
D) He is a believer

Key Takeaway
Al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila agree on the disbeliever's lack of divine support; their dispute is over the name, not the reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you recognize your dependence on Allah's tawfiq, or do you trust in your own strength? How does this lesson's distinction between naming and reality expose where your reliance truly lies?
?
The Mu'tazila and al-Husayn agreed that the disbeliever has no divine support. How does this make you examine the state of your own faith? Are you relying on Allah's 'isma and tawfiq, or taking His protection for granted?
?
Al-Husayn called the disbeliever's lack of support 'no power for faith'. What would you call your own moments of weakness? Is there a part of your life where you are being 'left to your own opinion'? What will you do to seek Allah's tawfiq?

The Meaning of Divine Will (Irada)
Maturidi discusses the meaning of irada and the shift to the creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila agree on the basic meaning of divine will as non-coercion, but diverge on whether actions are created by God.

Core Idea
The dispute over irada is really about the creation of actions, not the meaning of will.

Maturidi continues his analysis by turning to the concept of divine will (irada). Both al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila accept that God is not coerced, yet their understanding of how God's will relates to human actions leads to a deeper disagreement. This lesson unpacks that discussion and reveals the true point of contention.

Guided Path
1
Define irada
Al-Husayn defines irada as God not being overcome or coerced. Note the Mu'tazila accept this meaning.
2
Shift to creation of actions
Al-Husayn says actions are created; Mu'tazila deny that, so the real issue becomes creation of actions.
3
Al-Ka'bi's definition
Al-Ka'bi defines irada as God being a chooser (mukhtar) not overcome.
4
Contrast the positions
Al-Husayn affirms God's will in the beginning; Mu'tazila only affirm God's existence before creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر طرفًا مما عابت المعتزلة حسينا في النطق ووافقته في التحصيل. قال الحسين: الكافر وقت كفره ليست له قدرة الإيمان؛ وقدرة الإيمان عنده التوفيق والعصمة ووافقته المعتزلة على أنه ليس بمعصوم ولا موفق، بل هو مخذول متروك على رأيه، وذلك معنى قدرة الكفر عند الحسين. فاتفقا على المعنى الذي اختلفا في اسمه فحق المسألة بينهم في جعل التوفيق والعصمة قوة الإيمان والترك والخذلان قوة الكفر، لا [في] إفراد التكلم في القدرة والإغضاء عن حقيقة ما يجب القول به. وبالله التوفيق. وقال الحسين: معنى الإرادة في الله سبحانه أنه لم يغلب ولم يُقهر. وقد أعطت المعتزلة هذا المعنى في جميع أفعال الخلق: إنه لم يُغلب ولم يُقهر، فتبطل المسألة في الإرادة؛ إنما بقيت في تأويل الإرادة لا غير. مع ما كان من قول الحسين: «إن أفعال العباد مخلوقة، فأراد خالقها كونها على ما خلقها». ومذهب المعتزلة أنها ليست بمخلوقة لله، فتكون المسألة في خلق الأفعال لا في الإرادة. وقال الكعبي: الإرادة معناها أنه مختار غير مغلوب، فمثله في كل شيء يلزمه. ثم المعتزلة ليست تُثبت الله إلى العالم سوى أن كان ولم يكن عالَم، ثم كان عالم، فصار بذلك المعنى خالقا له، مريداً على الوجه الذي ذُكر. فقال الحسين في أفعال العباد: إنه إذ كان ولا هذه الأفعال، ثم كانت هذه، وكانت بإرادته التي تأويلها ما وصفه، وكان خلقها بأن كان ولم تكن هي. ولا قوة إلا بالله. على أن "الحسين" يجعله في الأول مريداً لكون الخلق على ما كان، وكذلك لكون كل مخلوق على ما كان بإرادته. والمعتزلة تنفي معنى الإرادة، لا تجعل غير أن كان ولم يكن الخلق ثم كان؛ فحق ذلك فيه أوجب. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقالت المعتزلة: الوعيد يأخذ من أخرجه فعله عن الإيمان، وكذلك قال الحسين وجميع أهل الإرجاء: أن كان من استحق بفعله زوال اسم الإيمان فهو كله في النار أبدا. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والاختلاف بين هؤلاء فيما به يخرج من الإيمان لا في حق الوعيد، فالاحتجاج بآي الوعيد في المسألة خطأ.
Then we mention a portion of what the Mu'tazila criticized al-Husayn for in wording but agreed with him in outcome. Al-Husayn said: 'The disbeliever at the time of his disbelief has no power for faith; and the power for faith with him is tawfiq (divine enablement) and 'isma (protection).' And the Mu'tazila agreed with him that he is not protected (ma'sum) nor enabled (muwaffaq), but rather he is forsaken (makhdhul) and left to his opinion, and that is the meaning of power for disbelief according to al-Husayn. So they agreed on the meaning on which they differed in name, so the truth of the issue between them lies in making tawfiq and 'isma the strength of faith, and abandonment (tark) and forsaking (khidhlan) the strength of disbelief, not in speaking separately about power and ignoring the reality of what must be said. And with Allah is success. And al-Husayn said: 'The meaning of irada (will) in God, exalted is He, is that He has not been overcome nor coerced.' And the Mu'tazila granted this meaning in all actions of creation: that He has not been overcome nor coerced, so the discussion about irada becomes invalid; it remains only in the interpretation of irada, nothing else. Along with what al-Husayn said: 'Indeed, the actions of servants are created, so their Creator willed their existence as He created them.' And the doctrine of the Mu'tazila is that they are not created by God, so the issue becomes about the creation of actions, not about irada. And al-Ka'bi said: 'Irada means that He is a chooser (mukhtar) not overcome (ghalib), so similarly in everything that follows for Him.' Then the Mu'tazila do not affirm [any relation] of God to the world except that He was and there was no world, then the world came to be, so by that meaning He is its Creator, willing in the manner mentioned. So al-Husayn said concerning the actions of servants: 'He was and these actions were not, then they came to be, and they came to be by His irada whose interpretation is what he described, and His creation of them is that He was and they were not.' And there is no power except with Allah. Moreover, al-Husayn makes Him in the beginning willing that creation be as it was, and likewise that every creature be as it was by His irada. And the Mu'tazila deny the meaning of irada, they do not posit anything except that He was and creation was not, then it came to be; so that makes the truth more binding in it. And there is no power except with Allah. And the Mu'tazila said: 'The threat (wa'id) applies to the one whose action removes him from faith.' And likewise al-Husayn and all the Murji'a said: 'Whoever by his action deserves the removal of the name of faith is entirely in the Fire forever.' And there is no power except with Allah. And the difference between these groups is concerning that by which one leaves faith, not concerning the right of the threat, so using verses of threat as evidence in the issue is wrong.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed analysis of the areas where al-Husayn (founder of al-Husayniyya) and the Mu'tazila agree in essence but differ in wording. Maturidi begins by citing the example of the disbeliever: al-Husayn says the disbeliever has no power for faith, and defines power for faith as tawfiq and 'isma. The Mu'tazila agree that the disbeliever is not ma'sum or muwaffaq, but rather makhdhul and left to his opinion. They agree on the meaning but differ on naming. The real issue is about what constitutes strength for faith or disbelief. Next, Maturidi discusses the meaning of irada (will) in God. Al-Husayn defines it as God not being overcome or coerced. The Mu'tazila accept this meaning for all actions of creation, so the dispute reduces to the interpretation of irada, not its reality. Al-Husayn further states that actions of servants are created and God willed them. The Mu'tazila deny that actions are created by God, shifting the dispute to the creation of actions rather than irada. Al-Ka'bi's definition of irada as 'chooser not overcome' is also noted. Maturidi then contrasts the views: the Mu'tazila only affirm God's existence before the world and His becoming Creator when the world comes to be, while al-Husayn extends divine will to the moment of creation. Regarding the divine threat (wa'id), both al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila agree that those who lose the name of faith by their actions will be eternally in Hell. However, their disagreement is about the specific actions that remove one from faith, not about the threat itself. Citing verses of threat is thus an error in this context.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
مختار (mukhtar)
مغلوب (maghlub)
خلق الأفعال (khalq al-af'al)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the dispute about irada is about the definition of will itself.
Correction: Maturidi shows that both sides agree on the basic meaning (non-coercion); the real dispute is about whether actions are created by God and whether will is an eternal attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on what do al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila agree regarding the meaning of irada?
A) It means creation
B) It means non-coercion
C) It means predestination
D) It means free will

Key Takeaway
The dispute over divine will is not about its basic meaning but about whether it entails the creation of human actions and whether it is an eternal attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your daily choices—your words, your actions, even your private thoughts—do you truly believe that God wills each one of them into existence? How does that change the way you approach mistakes or successes? What would it look like to live tomorrow as if every moment of your life is directly willed by God, not just permitted from afar?
?
The Mu'tazila were so concerned to protect God from being 'overcome' that they denied His direct creation of human actions. Are there areas in your life where you try to 'protect' God by limiting His control—for example, insisting on your own autonomy in areas you think are too small or too sinful for Him to be involved? How does that reflect a heart that trusts His sovereignty only in theory?
?
If God's will is eternal and not just a reaction to a changing world, then every event in your life has been eternally willed by Him. What would it mean for you to accept that even the disappointments, failures, and pains you face are part of that eternal will? Can you honestly say you would still praise Him for those things? Why or why not?

Divine Decree

The Divine Threat (Wa'id) and the Real Dispute
Maturidi clarifies the agreement on the threat and the real point of dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila agree on the eternal punishment for those who lose faith, but differ on the specific actions that cause removal from faith.

Core Idea
The real dispute about wa'id is over what removes one from faith, not over the threat itself.

In the final part of this passage, Maturidi addresses the topic of divine threat (wa'id). Both al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila, along with the Murji'a, agree that those who lose the name of faith by their actions will be eternally in Hell. However, the disagreement lies in identifying which actions cause that removal. Using verses of threat as evidence in this context is therefore misguided.

Guided Path
1
Read the agreement
Both sides say the person who loses faith by action will be in Hell forever.
2
Identify the real difference
The difference is about what constitutes leaving faith, not about the outcome.
3
Understand the error
Citing verses of threat is wrong because they address the outcome, not the cause.

Source Text

Commentary

According to Maturidi, what is the real difference between al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila regarding the threat?
A) Whether the threat is eternal
B) What actions remove one from faith
C) Whether the threat applies to believers
D) Whether God can forgive

Key Takeaway
Al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila agree on the eternal threat for those who lose faith; their dispute is about the cause of that loss.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses of threat in the Quran, do you immediately think of them applying to others or to yourself? Based on Maturidi's point, what error might you be making if you use them to judge others' faith?
?
The scholars debated what specific action removes one from faith. Have you ever taken the time to learn these criteria, or do you assume that a major sin automatically takes someone out of faith? How does that assumption affect how you view fellow Muslims?
?
If you knew that your own actions might have separated you from true faith, would you want to know? What concrete step will you take today to examine your own faith and ensure you are not among those who are merely confident but unaware of the real criteria?

The Quranic Verses on Universal Forgiveness
Maturidi cites Quranic verses as proof for universal forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quranic verses to establish that God's forgiveness encompasses both major and minor sins, refuting the Mu'tazila and Khawarij.

Core Idea
God's forgiveness is not limited to minor sins; the Quran proves it covers all.

Maturidi opens his refutation by citing three Quranic verses that show God's forgiveness applies to all sins, including major ones. These verses directly contradict the Mu'tazila view that only minor sins are forgiven without repentance and the Khawarij view that even minor sins incur eternal punishment. Let's examine the verses and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the verses and their translation
Read the Arabic verses listed and their English translation. Notice that each verse speaks of forgiveness for wrongdoing.
The verses are general in nature.
2
Identify the contradiction with the sects
Maturidi states that these verses show a recompense for both matters (major and minor sins), which is not the case according to the two sects. Think about why their views conflict with the verses.
3
Reflect on Maturidi's conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the verses indicate forgiveness for both types of sins. Ask yourself: How does this serve as a foundation for his later arguments?
This conclusion is the basis for refuting both sects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لقوله تعالى: (ومن يعمل سوءا أو يظلم نفسه الآية)، وقوله: (وإن ينتهوا يغفر لهم ما قد سلف) [الأنفال: 38]، وقال: (فأولئك يبدل الله سيئاتهم حسنات). دل ما ذكرت من تحقيق حال على أن فيها جزاء الأمرين، وذلك لا يكون على قول المعتزلة في وقت الكبائر ولا في وقت الصغائر، وكذا في قول الخوارج. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم الله تعالى قال: (إن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به الآية)، ومعلوم أن الشرك قد يغفر بالتوبة. فبطل به قول من يجعلها لما قسم الكتاب، وبطل قول من يبطل المغفرة في الكبائر بلا توبة، لأن الله جل ثناؤه جعل لنفسه مشيئة المغفرة، وذلك قد وجد فيما كان في الحكمة؛ فدفعه سفه، جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى؛ فلزم الذي ذكرت في القولين جميعا. ثم الذي ينقض قول الخوارج الذين يكفرون بالصغائر ما بلى به الأنبياء والأولياء، وما يكفر به يسقط النبوة والولاية. ومن ذلك وصف إيمانه بالأنبياء فهو كافر بهم. فبلغ من تعظيمهم الذنوب إلى أن كفروا به، وهو أعظم الذنوب. وهذا حق من تعدى حدود الله في الحكم وغلا في دين الله: أن يكون عطبه في أرجى ما يكون عنده من أسباب النجاة. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى قول المعتزلة في ذلك وصف الله الأنبياء بالدعاء له تضرعًا وخفية وطمعا وخوفا وببكائهم على ما كان منهم من الزلات وتضرعهم إليه حتى أجيبوا في دعائهم وأعطوا سؤلهم. ولو لم تكن ذنوبهم بحيث احتمال التعذيب عليها في الحكمة، أو كان عليهم من ذلك خوف التعذيب لكان في ذلك تعدى الحد والوصف بالجور والتعدي منه، وذلك أعظم من الزلات. فهذا ينفي قول المعتزلة في إثبات المغفرة في الصغائر وإخراج التعذيب عن فعل الحكمة، وقول الخوارج بإزالة اسم الإيمان عنه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
By His saying, the Exalted: 'And whoever does evil or wrongs himself [then seeks forgiveness]' and His saying: 'And if they desist, what is past will be forgiven them' and He said: 'Those, Allah will replace their evil deeds with good deeds.' What I mentioned of the verification of the state indicates that in it is the recompense of both matters, and that is not the case according to the Mu'tazila at the time of major sins nor at the time of minor sins, and similarly in the statement of the Khawarij. And there is no power except with Allah. Then Allah the Exalted said: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive associating partners with Him...' and it is known that shirk may be forgiven by repentance. So the saying of those who make [the division of sins according to] the division of the Book is invalidated, and the saying of those who nullify forgiveness for major sins without repentance is invalidated, because Allah, the Exalted, established for Himself the will of forgiveness, and that is found in what was [dictated by] wisdom; so rejecting it is foolishness, far is Allah from that and exalted; so what I mentioned in both statements is necessary. Then what refutes the statement of the Khawarij who declare disbelief for minor sins is what prophets and saints are afflicted with, and what leads to disbelief [about them] nullifies prophethood and sainthood. And among that is describing belief in the prophets as being a disbeliever in them. So their magnification of sins reached the point that they disbelieved in Him, and that is the greatest of sins. And this is truly from one who transgresses the limits of Allah in judgment and exceeds in the religion of Allah: that his destruction comes from the most hopeful means of salvation with him. And there is no power except with Allah. And according to the Mu'tazila in that, [they described] Allah's prophets supplicating to Him humbly and secretly and in hope and fear, and their weeping over what occurred from them of slips, and their supplication to Him until they were answered in their supplication and were granted their request. And if their sins were not such that the possibility of punishment upon them was [dictated by] wisdom, or if they had fear of punishment from that, then that would be transgression of the limit and description of injustice and transgression from Him, and that is greater than the slips. This then negates the statement of the Mu'tazila in affirming forgiveness for minor sins and removing punishment from the act of wisdom, and the statement of the Khawarij in removing the name of faith from him. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of both the Mu'tazila and the Khawarij on the issue of forgiveness of sins. Maturidi begins by citing several Quranic verses that indicate God forgives sins, including major sins, without specifying repentance as a condition. He argues that the Mu'tazila's view that only minor sins are forgiven without repentance (and major sins require repentance or punishment) is contradicted by these verses. Similarly, the Khawarij's view that even minor sins lead to eternal punishment or disbelief is refuted. He then provides a specific argument against the Khawarij: if minor sins cause disbelief, then prophets who committed minor sins (like slips) would be disbelievers, which is absurd. Against the Mu'tazila, he argues that if prophets' minor sins could be punished, then their fear of punishment would be unjust, and God would be described as unjust. Thus, the Mu'tazila position leads to attributing injustice to God, which is a greater fault than the sins themselves. The passage concludes that both positions are invalid.

Key Terms
الكبائر
الصغائر
المغفرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that forgiveness is only for minor sins or requires repentance.
Correction: Maturidi shows the Quranic verses indicate broader forgiveness.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do the three Quranic verses indicate about forgiveness?
A) Only minor sins are forgiven
B) Only major sins are forgiven with repentance
C) Both major and minor sins are forgiven
D) No sins are forgiven without punishment

Key Takeaway
Quranic verses prove that God's forgiveness includes both major and minor sins, refuting the selective forgiveness of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that a sin you committed is too great for God to forgive? What does this lesson reveal about the scope of God's mercy? How will you change your view of His forgiveness today?
?
The Mu'tazila and Khawarij placed conditions on God's forgiveness. Do you find yourself placing hidden conditions in your heart on who or what God can forgive? Will you accept that God's mercy is wider than your own judgments?
?
If God can replace your evil deeds with good ones, what does that mean for your past mistakes? What is one step you will take today to actively seek that transformation in your own life?

The Verse on Shirk and God's Freedom to Forgive
Maturidi uses the shirk verse to prove God's unconditional will to forgive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the verse on shirk to argue that God's will to forgive is unrestricted, refuting those who limit forgiveness.

Core Idea
Even shirk can be forgiven, so God's forgiveness is by His will, not by human merit.

After establishing the Quranic evidence, Maturidi turns to the verse that is often used to restrict forgiveness: "God does not forgive shirk..." He argues that this verse actually supports God's free will in forgiveness. Let's see his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic verse and Maturidi's commentary
Read the line: 'Then Allah said: Indeed, Allah does not forgive associating partners with Him... and it is known that shirk may be forgiven by repentance.' Notice the key point: shirk itself is forgiven by repentance.
2
Understand the refutation of both sects
Maturidi says this invalidates those who divide sins into two categories (major and minor) and those who deny forgiveness for major sins without repentance. Why? Because if shirk is forgiven by repentance, then lesser sins are even more likely to be forgiven.
This refutes both Mu'tazila and Khawarij.
3
Reflect on the concept of God's will
Maturidi says 'God has established for Himself the will of forgiveness.' This means forgiveness is based on His will, not on a condition. Ask: What does this say about God's sovereignty?
It emphasizes God's absolute freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لقوله تعالى: (ومن يعمل سوءا أو يظلم نفسه الآية)، وقوله: (وإن ينتهوا يغفر لهم ما قد سلف) [الأنفال: 38]، وقال: (فأولئك يبدل الله سيئاتهم حسنات). دل ما ذكرت من تحقيق حال على أن فيها جزاء الأمرين، وذلك لا يكون على قول المعتزلة في وقت الكبائر ولا في وقت الصغائر، وكذا في قول الخوارج. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم الله تعالى قال: (إن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به الآية)، ومعلوم أن الشرك قد يغفر بالتوبة. فبطل به قول من يجعلها لما قسم الكتاب، وبطل قول من يبطل المغفرة في الكبائر بلا توبة، لأن الله جل ثناؤه جعل لنفسه مشيئة المغفرة، وذلك قد وجد فيما كان في الحكمة؛ فدفعه سفه، جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى؛ فلزم الذي ذكرت في القولين جميعا. ثم الذي ينقض قول الخوارج الذين يكفرون بالصغائر ما بلى به الأنبياء والأولياء، وما يكفر به يسقط النبوة والولاية. ومن ذلك وصف إيمانه بالأنبياء فهو كافر بهم. فبلغ من تعظيمهم الذنوب إلى أن كفروا به، وهو أعظم الذنوب. وهذا حق من تعدى حدود الله في الحكم وغلا في دين الله: أن يكون عطبه في أرجى ما يكون عنده من أسباب النجاة. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى قول المعتزلة في ذلك وصف الله الأنبياء بالدعاء له تضرعًا وخفية وطمعا وخوفا وببكائهم على ما كان منهم من الزلات وتضرعهم إليه حتى أجيبوا في دعائهم وأعطوا سؤلهم. ولو لم تكن ذنوبهم بحيث احتمال التعذيب عليها في الحكمة، أو كان عليهم من ذلك خوف التعذيب لكان في ذلك تعدى الحد والوصف بالجور والتعدي منه، وذلك أعظم من الزلات. فهذا ينفي قول المعتزلة في إثبات المغفرة في الصغائر وإخراج التعذيب عن فعل الحكمة، وقول الخوارج بإزالة اسم الإيمان عنه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
By His saying, the Exalted: 'And whoever does evil or wrongs himself [then seeks forgiveness]' and His saying: 'And if they desist, what is past will be forgiven them' and He said: 'Those, Allah will replace their evil deeds with good deeds.' What I mentioned of the verification of the state indicates that in it is the recompense of both matters, and that is not the case according to the Mu'tazila at the time of major sins nor at the time of minor sins, and similarly in the statement of the Khawarij. And there is no power except with Allah. Then Allah the Exalted said: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive associating partners with Him...' and it is known that shirk may be forgiven by repentance. So the saying of those who make [the division of sins according to] the division of the Book is invalidated, and the saying of those who nullify forgiveness for major sins without repentance is invalidated, because Allah, the Exalted, established for Himself the will of forgiveness, and that is found in what was [dictated by] wisdom; so rejecting it is foolishness, far is Allah from that and exalted; so what I mentioned in both statements is necessary. Then what refutes the statement of the Khawarij who declare disbelief for minor sins is what prophets and saints are afflicted with, and what leads to disbelief [about them] nullifies prophethood and sainthood. And among that is describing belief in the prophets as being a disbeliever in them. So their magnification of sins reached the point that they disbelieved in Him, and that is the greatest of sins. And this is truly from one who transgresses the limits of Allah in judgment and exceeds in the religion of Allah: that his destruction comes from the most hopeful means of salvation with him. And there is no power except with Allah. And according to the Mu'tazila in that, [they described] Allah's prophets supplicating to Him humbly and secretly and in hope and fear, and their weeping over what occurred from them of slips, and their supplication to Him until they were answered in their supplication and were granted their request. And if their sins were not such that the possibility of punishment upon them was [dictated by] wisdom, or if they had fear of punishment from that, then that would be transgression of the limit and description of injustice and transgression from Him, and that is greater than the slips. This then negates the statement of the Mu'tazila in affirming forgiveness for minor sins and removing punishment from the act of wisdom, and the statement of the Khawarij in removing the name of faith from him. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of both the Mu'tazila and the Khawarij on the issue of forgiveness of sins. Maturidi begins by citing several Quranic verses that indicate God forgives sins, including major sins, without specifying repentance as a condition. He argues that the Mu'tazila's view that only minor sins are forgiven without repentance (and major sins require repentance or punishment) is contradicted by these verses. Similarly, the Khawarij's view that even minor sins lead to eternal punishment or disbelief is refuted. He then provides a specific argument against the Khawarij: if minor sins cause disbelief, then prophets who committed minor sins (like slips) would be disbelievers, which is absurd. Against the Mu'tazila, he argues that if prophets' minor sins could be punished, then their fear of punishment would be unjust, and God would be described as unjust. Thus, the Mu'tazila position leads to attributing injustice to God, which is a greater fault than the sins themselves. The passage concludes that both positions are invalid.

Key Terms
الشرك
المغفرة
التوبة
الكبائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse 'God does not forgive shirk' means shirk is never forgiven.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that it is forgiven through repentance.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi use to refute the claim that major sins cannot be forgiven without repentance?
A) The fact that shirk can be forgiven by repentance
B) The prophets' slips
C) The consensus of scholars
D) The Khawarij's view

Key Takeaway
The shirk verse actually proves God's freedom to forgive any sin, including shirk, by His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of your worst sin, do you secretly believe it is too big for God to forgive unless you perform a perfect repentance? How does Maturidi's argument—that even shirk can be forgiven by God's will—expose that lie and call you to trust in His mercy instead of your own efforts?
?
If you truly believed that God's forgiveness depends entirely on His will, not on your repentance record, would you still avoid sin? Or would you fall into presumption? What does your answer reveal about the state of your heart regarding God's majesty and grace?
?
The verse 'God does not forgive shirk' is often used to frighten. But Maturidi says it proves God's freedom to forgive. Are you living in fear or hope? Will you today ask God for forgiveness for a sin you have hidden, not because you deserve it, but because He wills to forgive? What is holding you back?

Refutation of the Khawarij: Minor Sins and Prophethood
Maturidi refutes Khawarij by using prophets' minor sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if minor sins cause disbelief, then prophets who committed minor slips would not be prophets, which is absurd.

Core Idea
The Khawarij's extreme view on minor sins leads to the denial of prophethood.

Maturidi now directly targets the Khawarij, who believe that committing minor sins results in disbelief and eternal punishment. He uses the example of prophets and saints, who are known to have committed minor mistakes, to show the absurdity of this position. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Khawarij
Read the Arabic: 'Then what refutes the statement of the Khawarij who declare disbelief for minor sins is what prophets and saints are afflicted with.' Notice the key word 'afflicted with' – prophets had slips.
2
Understand the logical consequence
If minor sins cause disbelief, then prophets would be disbelievers, which is false. So the Khawarij's premise is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the irony
Maturidi says the Khawarij magnified sins to the point they disbelieved in God (by accusing prophets). This shows their extremism leads to greater sin.
Their strictness leads to the greatest sin.

?

Refutation of the Mu'tazila: Prophets' Supplication and God's Justice
Maturidi uses prophets' fear to refute Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if prophets' minor sins were punishable, then their fear of punishment would make God unjust, which is impossible.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's view on minor sins implies attributing injustice to God.

Finally, Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila by examining the behavior of prophets. The Mu'tazila affirm forgiveness for minor sins but claim they are subject to punishment if not repented. However, prophets feared punishment for their slips, which would be unjust if the sins were not punishable by wisdom. Maturidi shows this leads to a logical problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Mu'tazila
Read the Arabic: 'And according to the Mu'tazila, [they described] Allah's prophets supplicating to Him humbly... and their weeping over their slips.' Notice the emotional response.
2
Understand the conditional argument
Maturidi says: If the prophets' sins were not punishable, then their fear would be unjust. But we know prophets are wise. So the Mu'tazila must be wrong.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Conclude the refutation
The final line says this negates the Mu'tazila's position on forgiveness of minor sins and the Khawarij's removal of faith. So the argument refutes both.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that minor sins are automatically forgiven or not punishable.
Correction: Maturidi shows that prophets feared punishment, indicating they are punishable.

Checkpoint
What do the prophets' supplications and fear indicate about minor sins according to Maturidi?
A) They are automatically forgiven
B) They are not punishable
C) They are punishable according to God's wisdom
D) They are not sins

Key Takeaway
The prophets' fear of punishment for their slips proves that minor sins are subject to God's wisdom regarding punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin you consider 'minor', does your heart feel the same fear of Allah's punishment as the prophets did for their slips? If not, what does that reveal about your understanding of Allah's justice and your own accountability?
?
This lesson shows that even the prophets feared the consequences of their minor sins. How does that challenge the common assumption that 'small sins' are trivial or easily forgiven without repentance? What would it mean for your daily life to treat every sin with the gravity that the prophets did?
?
Maturidi refutes the idea that Allah's wisdom excludes punishment for minor sins. In what specific ways have you excused your own sins by telling yourself 'Allah will understand' or 'it's not that bad'? How will you change your attitude toward repentance starting today?

Like-for-Like Recompense for the Obstinate
Maturidi argues that like-for-like for the obstinate is khulud, using Q6:160.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that eternal punishment is the just recompense for obstinate disbelief, based on Qur'an 6:160.

Core Idea
For the obstinate, the just like-for-like recompense is eternal punishment.

This lesson examines Imam Maturidi's rational argument for why eternal punishment is specific to the obstinate disbeliever. He begins with the Qur'anic principle that evil is only recompensed with its like, and then shows that a temporary punishment with eventual relief would be chosen by the obstinate, making it plain that only eternal punishment matches the gravity of their sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage aloud, then follow the literal translation. Notice how Maturidi starts with a Qur'anic verse and builds his argument.
The key term is 'al-kafir al-mu'anid' (obstinate disbeliever).
2
Analyze the Qur'anic basis
Maturidi cites Q6:160—'whoever comes with an evil deed will only be recompensed with its like.' Consider how this principle applies to the worst evil: persistent disbelief.
3
Understand the thought experiment
Imagine the obstinate disbeliever being offered all punishments combined but with eventual deliverance. Maturidi says they would accept that—so the 'like' of their sin must be eternal.
This shows that the like is not temporary but eternal.
4
Connect to divine wisdom
If a lesser sinner were given the same eternal punishment, it would be more than the like, contradicting wisdom. This confirms that khulud is specific to obstinate disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيدخل فيه أمران أحدهما الكذب في الوعد حيث قال: ومن جاء بالسيئة فلا يُجزى إلا مثلها. ومعلوم أن الكافر المعاند لو جمع عليه جميع ما يعاقب به على شرط النجاة والراحة يحتمله ويختاره. فثبت أن مثل سيئته هو الخلود في العذاب؛ فإذا لا يعاقب بما يرتكب وهو عذب بمثله من كان ذنبه دونه كان جازيًا أكثر من مثله، وفي الحكمة عقوبته، وهذا مما يمنع الحكمة. والله الموفق.
Then there enter into it two matters: first is the truthfulness in the promise when He said: 'And whoever comes with an evil deed will not be recompensed except with its like.' And it is known that the obstinate disbeliever, if all that he is punished with were gathered together on condition of deliverance and comfort, he would bear it and choose it. So it is established that the like of his evil deed is eternal punishment in the Fire. Therefore, if he is not punished for what he commits, and he is punished with its like, then one whose sin is less than him would be recompensed more than its like, and in wisdom his punishment, and this is something that prevents wisdom. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage is the first of two matters (amran) that Maturidi introduces. He cites the Qur'anic verse (6:160) that evil is only recompensed with its like. For the obstinate disbeliever (al-kafir al-mu'anid), if all possible punishments were gathered together but with a condition of eventual deliverance and comfort, he would choose to bear that punishment rather than give up his obstinacy. Therefore, the 'like' of his sin is eternal punishment (khulud). If God were to punish a lesser sinner (dunu) with the same eternal punishment, He would be recompensing more than the like of the lesser sin, which contradicts wisdom (hikma). Thus, such treatment would be unjust and contrary to divine wisdom.

Key Terms
الكافر المعاند
الخلود في العذاب
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal punishment is the same for all sins.
Correction: Maturidi shows that eternal punishment is specific to the obstinate disbeliever; a lesser sinner would receive a punishment corresponding to the 'like' of their sin, not necessarily eternal.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi identify as the 'like' of the obstinate disbeliever's sin?
A) Temporary punishment
B) Eternal punishment
C) Forgiveness
D) Disbelief itself

Key Takeaway
For the obstinate disbeliever, the just recompense is eternal punishment, because only that matches the 'like' of their sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you harboring any stubbornness against a truth you know from Allah? What would it cost you to submit to it today?
?
God's promise is true: your every evil deed will be repaid with its like. What evil have you normalized that you need to repent from right now?
?
The obstinate disbeliever chose temporary comfort over eternal truth. Where in your life are you choosing comfort over obedience? What will you change starting today?

The Verse of Fighting Believers: Faith and Major Sins
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazilite interpretation of Surah al-Hujurat verses 9-10.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that major sins expel faith by analyzing the Qur'anic verse about fighting believers.

Core Idea
Even grave sinners remain believers in this world.

Imam Maturidi takes on a key proof text used by the Mu'tazilites to argue that major sins negate faith. They pointed to the verse where believers fight each other, claiming that such sinners cannot truly be believers. Maturidi shows that the verse itself calls them 'believers' and that the command to fight the transgressor actually proves that their sin is major—yet the name of faith remains. This refutation is central to the Maturidi position that faith is not nullified by sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage provided twice—once to get the flow, then again to note the specific arguments Maturidi makes. Focus on the analogy with the apostasy verse and the discussion of ijtihad.
The passage is dense; take your time with the translation.
2
Analyze the apostasy analogy
Maturidi compares the verse to another verse (2:217) where someone who apostatizes was called a believer before. Similarly, the fighting verse calls them believers despite the sin. Notice how this analogy undercuts the Mu'tazilite position.
3
Understand the ijtihad and weaponless fighting argument
Maturidi considers the possibility that the fighting was without weapons (e.g., wrestling) or based on mistaken ijtihad. Even if so, the sin might be minor—but the command to fight the transgressor shows it was major, yet they still kept the name of faith.
This strengthens the point that major sins do not remove faith.
4
Reflect on the brotherhood verse
The passage ends by referencing the verse 'The believers are but brothers' (49:10) as further proof that the fighting believers remain part of the brotherhood of faith. Reflect on how this verse reinforces the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أجاب في قوله : وإن طائفتان من المؤمنين اقتتلوا الآية، أنه كقوله : ومن يرتدد منكم عن دينه وقد كان سماه مؤمنا من قبل والثاني أن يكون الاقتتال بغير سلاح نحو المجاذبة، أو كانوا مجتهدين فلا يخرجون به من الإيمان. فيقال: إذ جرى الأمر بالإصلاح بينهم وتسمية الإخوة بطل معنى الردّة. وقوله : فقاتلوا التي تبغى [الحجرات، ٩/٤٩] دل أن الباغي كان معلوماً، لا أن كان ثمة اجتهاد. مع ما كان رسول الله بين أظهرهم أنّى لهم الاجتهاد إلى ذلك الحد؟ ثم دل الأمر بالقتال ـ والصغيرة تكون مغفورة لا يقابل عليها - على أن ذنوبهم قد كبرت، وقد أبقى الله لهم اسم الإيمان. والله الموفق. وقال في قوله : إنما المؤمنون / إخوة ﴾ [الحجرات، ١٠/٤٩] بمثل ذلك، وقد بينا وهمه .
Then he responded regarding His saying: 'And if two groups of believers fight each other, make peace between them' (the verse), that it is like His saying: 'And whoever of you turns back from his religion and dies while a disbeliever...' (2:217) and He had called him a believer before. And second, that the fighting could be without weapons, like wrestling, or they were engaged in ijtihad, so they do not exit faith by it. It is said: Since the command to make peace between them and the naming of brotherhood occurred, the meaning of apostasy is nullified. And His saying: 'Then fight the one that transgresses' (49:9) indicates that the transgressor was known, not that there was ijtihad. Moreover, while the Messenger of Allah was among them, how could they have ijtihad to that extent? Then the command to fight—while minor sins are forgiven and not punished—indicates that their sins had become major, yet Allah preserved for them the name of faith. And Allah is the granter of success. And he said regarding His saying: 'The believers are but brothers' (49:10) similarly, and we have shown his error.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite interpretation of the Qur'anic verse about believers fighting each other. The author argues that the verse itself uses the term 'believers' (مؤمنين) for those who fight, so they are still believers despite the sin. He compares this to the verse on apostasy where the person was called a believer before. He then addresses the possibility that the fighting was without weapons or due to ijtihad, but argues that the command to fight the transgressor indicates the sin was major, yet they retain the name. He concludes with the brotherhood verse, reinforcing the same point.

Key Terms
مؤمن (believer)
كبيرة (major sin)
إيمان (faith)
ردّة (apostasy)
اقتتال (fighting)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse shows that grave sinners are not believers because they fight.
Correction: The verse explicitly calls them 'believers' even as they fight, proving that major sins do not remove the name of faith in this world.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's argument, what does the verse 'And if two groups of believers fight...' prove about grave sinners?
A) They are not believers because they fight.
B) They are still called believers despite committing major sins.
C) They must be considered apostates.
D) The verse only applies to minor sins.

Key Takeaway
The verse confirms that believers are called believers even when committing major sins, disproving the Mu'tazilite view that grave sins expel faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin you know is major, do you still see yourself as a believer with the same certainty as before? Or do you feel your faith has diminished? What does Allah's act of preserving the name of faith for the fighting believers tell you about His mercy and your own worth before Him?
?
The verse calls Muslims brothers even as they fight each other. Is there a person you are in conflict with whom you find it hard to see as a brother or sister in faith? How does this lesson challenge you to maintain that bond despite disagreement or hurt?
?
If Allah commands peace between believers even after major sin, what does that imply about the possibility of reconciliation in your own relationships? Are you willing to initiate peace, or do you hold onto resentment? What step will you take today to embody the brotherhood affirmed in this verse?

Persistence and Minor Sins: Refuting the Mu'tazila
Refutes the Mu'tazila claim that persistence in minor sins makes one a committer of major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why persistence in a minor sin does not make it a major sin according to Imam Maturidi, and how the Quranic distinctions between shirk, major sins, and minor sins are preserved.

Core Idea
Persistence is not continuing the act, but failing to repent.

The Mu'tazila argued that persisting in a minor sin elevates it to a major sin. But does persistence mean repeating the same act, or something else? Imam Maturidi explains that true persistence is the refusal to repent and regret. This teaching not only clarifies the nature of sin but also safeguards the Quran's clear gradation of sins. Let's read his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: the Mu'tazila claim, the definition of persistence, then the Quranic refutation.
2
Identify the definition of persistence
Find where the author defines what persistence (iṣrār) really means. Is it about continuing the sin or something else?
The author says persistence is not 'luzūm' (continuance) of the act.
3
Understand the Quranic implications
The passage mentions two verses: 4:48 (shirk vs other sins) and 4:31 (major vs minor sins). See how the Mu'tazila position would nullify the distinction in these verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قالت المعتزلة: صاحب الصغيرة إذا أصر عليها يصير صاحب كبيرة. والإصرار على ذلك الفعل ليس هو لزومه، لأنه لا فعلَ يمكن لزومه حتى لا يتحول منه إلى غيره. فليس إذا الإصرار إلا ترك التوبة والندامة عليه، وكل الذنوب من الشرك وغيره مغفور بالتوبة عنها والندامة عليها. فيبطل على قولهم حق هذه الآية من التفصيل بين الشرك وما دونه، وحق الآية الأخرى من التفصيل بين الكبائر وما دونها، ويحصل القول على أن كل ذنب يوجب الخلود إلا أن يتاب عنه، وذلك بيّن لمن تأمله ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the Muʿtazila said: 'The one who commits a minor sin, if he persists in it, he becomes a committer of a major sin.' Persistence (iṣrār) regarding that act is not its continuance, because there is no act which can be continued such that one cannot turn from it to something else. So persistence is nothing other than abandoning repentance (tawba) and regret (nadāma) over it. And all sins, from shirk to others, are forgiven through repentance and regret over them. Thus, according to their statement, the right of this verse (4:48) regarding differentiation between shirk and what is less than it is invalidated, and the right of the other verse (4:31) regarding differentiation between major sins and what is less than them is invalidated. And the statement results that every sin necessitates eternal [punishment] unless one repents from it. That is clear for anyone who reflects upon it. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila doctrine that persistence in a minor sin turns it into a major sin. The author argues that persistence (iṣrār) does not mean continuing the same act, because acts can be ceased. Instead, persistence is simply failing to repent and regret. All sins, including shirk, are forgiven through repentance. If the Mu'tazila claim that persistence makes one a committer of major sins, then the clear differentiation in Quran 4:48 (between shirk and other sins) and 4:31 (between major sins and minor sins) would be undermined. Their position would imply that every unrepented sin leads to eternal punishment, which contradicts the Quran's gradation of sins.

Key Terms
إصرار
صغيرة
كبيرة
توبة
ندامة
خلود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Persistence means physically repeating or continuing the same sin.
Correction: The author teaches that persistence is not about repeating the act, but about failing to repent and regret the sin, which is a state of the heart.

Checkpoint
What does the author define as persistence (iṣrār) in a minor sin?
A) Repeating the same act numerous times.
B) Committing the sin continuously without interruption.
C) Abandoning repentance and regret over the sin.
D) Intending to commit the sin again.

Key Takeaway
Persistence in sin is defined by the absence of repentance, not by repetition; therefore, repentance removes the sin's severity, preserving the Quranic gradation of sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a sin you have been committing repeatedly, telling yourself 'it's just small,' but without genuine repentance? What does this lesson reveal about your state before Allah?
?
The Mu'tazila's error led to despair by making all unrepented sins threaten eternal punishment. Do you ever fall into despair over your sins, forgetting that repentance wipes them away regardless of repetition? How will you embrace tawba today?
?
When you sin, do you immediately feel regret and turn to Allah, or do you postpone repentance? What do you need to change in your heart so that you never allow yourself to be 'persisting' by delaying tawba?

Is Every Sin Obedience to Shaitan?
Objection that all sin is obedience to shaitan and thus kufr, with start of Maturidi's reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that all sins might constitute obedience to shaitan and thus disbelief, and why this objection is problematic.

Core Idea
Not all sins are worship of shaitan; intention matters.

In this passage, an unnamed objector challenges the definition of obedience to shaitan. If every act contrary to Allah is something shaitan calls to and delights in, then every sin would be an act of obedience to shaitan, potentially making all sinners disbelievers or worshippers of shaitan. Imam Maturidi begins his reply by noting that this objection does not apply to the Khawarij and Mu'tazila because of their own principles. The full answer is cut off, but we can analyze the objection and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the objector's statement. Notice the logical chain: all sin → shaitan calls to it → delight → therefore obedience → therefore kufr/worship.
Pay attention to the terms used: ṭāʿa, ʿibāda, kufr.
2
Identify the premises and conclusion
Break down the argument: Premise 1: Every sin is something shaitan calls to and delights in. Premise 2: Doing something shaitan calls to is obedience to him. Premise 3: Obedience to shaitan is kufr or worship of shaitan. Conclusion: Every sin is kufr or worship of shaitan.
The objector uses the concept of 'sharʿ' (law) to equate setting up a rival law with worship.
3
Consider Maturidi's opening reply
Imam Maturidi says the question does not apply to the Khawarij and Mu'tazila because they affirm... (the text cuts off). Think about why those groups would not face this problem. What do they believe about faith and sin?
Hint: Khawarij consider major sins as kufr, and Mu'tazila consider them as grave sins without kufr, but both have specific definitions of obedience.
4
Reflect on the missing answer
The source cuts off. Imagine how Maturidi would complete the answer. He would likely distinguish between acts that are merely in line with shaitan's call vs. deliberate worship with intent.
Consider the broader Maturidi position: sin does not automatically negate iman unless it involves shirk or rejection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قائل: إذ كان كل خلاف الله فهو مما دعا إليه الشيطان ويُسَرّ به لو فعل، لم لا صار ذلك طاعة له؟ ومن فعل فعلاً لطاعة الشيطان يكفر أو يصير به عابدا له، إذ ذلك منه وضع شرع مقابل لشرع الله ودعوة إليه. ومن عبد الشيطان فقد بين الله منازل عُبّاد الشيطان. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: نقول: ليست هذه المسألة للخوارج والمعتزلة، لإقرارهم في...
And someone said: 'Since every act contrary to Allah is something to which shaitan calls and he would be delighted if it were done, why would that not become obedience (ṭāʿa) to him? And whoever does an act in obedience to shaitan disbelieves or becomes a worshipper of him through it, because that is from him setting up a law (sharʿ) in opposition to the law of Allah and a call to it. And whoever worships shaitan, Allah has explained the stations of the worshippers of shaitan.' Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'We say: This question does not apply to the Khawarij and the Muʿtazila, because they affirm in...'
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the beginning of an answer. The objector argues: if every sin is something shaitan calls to and delights in, then any sin would be considered obedience to shaitan. Performing an act in obedience to shaitan would then constitute disbelief (kufr) or worship of shaitan, since it involves setting up a rival law. The objector implies a problematic conclusion: all sinners are worshippers of shaitan. Abu Mansur (Imam Maturidi) begins his reply by noting that this objection does not apply to the Khawarij and Mu'tazila because they affirm... (the text cuts off). The implication is that these groups have other principles that prevent this conclusion, such as their definitions of faith and obedience. The full answer would likely clarify that not every act pleasing to shaitan constitutes worship; worship requires intent and recognition.

Key Terms
خلاف الله
طاعة الشيطان
عبادة
كفر
شرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any sin is an act of worship of shaitan and thus tantamount to kufr.
Correction: Not all sins are acts of worship; worship requires intention and recognition of the object of worship. The objector's conclusion is extreme and not upheld by mainstream theology.

Checkpoint
What is the objector's main concern in this passage?
A) That all sins are equal in punishment.
B) That every sin could be considered obedience to shaitan and thus kufr.
C) That minor sins are not forgivable.
D) That shaitan only calls to major sins.

Key Takeaway
Not every sin constitutes obedience or worship of shaitan; distinct intentions and theological frameworks prevent the extreme conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever tell yourself, 'This is just a small mistake'? But the objector says every sin is obedience to shaitan. Does that thought make you uneasy? How does the distinction between intention and mere action (hinted in Maturidi's reply) change how you look at your own sins?
?
The objector claims that because shaitan delights in your sin, it becomes worship of him. Have you ever felt enslaved by a sin, as if you are obeying shaitan? What would it take for you to intentionally choose obedience to Allah over the delight of shaitan, starting with the next temptation?
?
Maturidi begins his reply by saying the objection doesn't apply to certain groups. Have you ever used a theological loophole to justify a sin? How does knowing that your intention and theological framework (e.g., your understanding of faith) matter make you more accountable for not just the act but the heart behind it?
