Proof for Divine Creation and Human Acquisition of Actions
Maturidi provides a rational proof for the necessity of affirming both God's creation and human acquisition of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational proof that human actions are created by God from one aspect and acquired by humans from another, and why rejecting this leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
Actions have two aspects: one beyond human power, one within it.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a sophisticated rational argument for the Ash'ari/Maturidi position on human acts. He identifies two distinct sets of states within every human action: those that transcend human comprehension and capacity, and those that fall within human intention and ability. By analyzing these, he demonstrates that actions are simultaneously from God (in their creative origin) and from humans (in their acquisition). The argument culminates in an apagogic proof that if humans had full creative power, the evidence for God and prophets would be undermined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects of actions
Read the first part of the passage where Maturidi states there are 'states' in actions beyond human imagination and states within human intention. Notice how he defines each and gives examples.
The first aspect includes bringing something from non-existence to existence; the second includes movement and stillness.
2
Examine the hypothetical argument
Consider Maturidi's conditional reasoning: if humans could fully create their actions (the first aspect), then it would be possible for the universe and miracles to be from an ignorant, powerless source. Since this is not the case, humans cannot have full creative power.
This is an apagogic argument (reduction to absurdity).
3
Connect to the Quranic verse
Reflect on the verse 'There is nothing like Him' (42:11) and how it supports the distinction: God's creative power is unique, while human acts share similarity from the acquisitive aspect but not from the creative aspect.
This grounds the rational proof in revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل على لزوم القول بهذا مع ما فيما بينا كفاية وجود أحوال في أفعال العبد لا يبلغها أوهامهم ولا يقدرها عقولهم، وأحوال فيها ينتهي إليها قصدهم وتبلغها عقولهم، فثبت أنها من الوجه الأول ليست لهم، ومن الوجه الثاني لهم. فالأول كتصوير خروج الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود وكأخذ الفعل من قدر الجو والمكان والحد الذي لو أحب أن يعود إليه ما أمكنه بلا فيه، والثاني نحو التحرك والسكون بالمنهي والمأمور به، ثبت من الوجه الأول ليس لهم، ومن الثاني لهم. ولو جاز تحقيق فعلهم من الوجه أن فعلهم من الأول على ظهور خروجه من قصدهم وجملتهم مختلفة مما ذكر، وعجزهم عن العود إلى مثله لجاز كون العالم على ما عليه بمن لا يقدر ولا يعلم ولا يعرف مقادير كل شيء، ويجوز أيضا آيات على ما هي عليه بالبشر وإن لم يكن بمثلها علم ولا عليها قدرة. فإذ ألزمهم القول بالصانع والرسل بخروج الذي ذكرت عن وسع الخلق فمثله أفعال الخلق؛ ولذلك قال الله سبحانه: لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ وأوجب أن تشابه الخلق من الوجه الذي قلت تماثلا، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then the proof for the necessity of saying this, along with what we have explained, is the sufficiency of the existence of states in the actions of the servant that their imaginations do not reach nor their intellects measure, and states in which their intention ends and their intellects attain. So it is established that from the first aspect they are not for them, and from the second aspect they are for them. The first is like the formation of a thing's going from non-existence to existence, and like taking the action from the capacity of the air, place, and limit which if he wanted to return to it, he would not be able to without it. The second is like movement and stillness in terms of what is prohibited and commanded. It is established that from the first aspect it is not for them, and from the second it is for them. And if it were permissible to verify their action from the aspect that their action from the first is on the appearance of its going out from their intention and their entirety being different from what was mentioned, and their inability to return to the like of it, then it would be permissible for the world to be as it is from one who does not have power, knowledge, nor knows the measures of everything, and also permissible for the signs (miracles) to be as they are from humans even if they have no knowledge of their like nor power over them. So since they are compelled to the statement of the Maker and the messengers by the coming out of what I mentioned from the capacity of creation, then similarly [is] the actions of creation. And therefore God Almighty said: 'There is nothing like Him,' and He necessitated that creation resembles [Him] from the aspect which you said [prevents] similarity. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 828

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof for the necessity of holding that actions are from one aspect not from humans and from another aspect from them. Maturidi identifies two categories of states in human actions: (1) states beyond human comprehension and capacity, such as bringing a thing from non-existence into existence or performing an action within the specific capacity of time, space, and limit such that if one tried to revert it, one could not; (2) states within human intention and understanding, such as performing commanded or prohibited movements and stillness. From the first aspect, actions are not from humans; from the second, they are. He then argues hypothetically: if humans were fully capable of the first aspect (origination), then it would be permissible for the world to exist from an ignorant, powerless being, and for miracles to be performed by humans without knowledge or power. Since the opponents (Qadariyya) are compelled to affirm a wise Creator and prophets precisely because of these signs that are beyond human capacity, the same reasoning applies to ordinary actions: they too must be beyond human creative power from one aspect. Thus, actions are created by God (in the first aspect) and acquired by humans (in the second). The Quranic verse 'There is nothing like Him' (42:11) supports this by negating any similarity between Creator and creation in the aspect of creative power, while affirming that actions are attributed to humans in a different aspect.

Key Terms

الوجه
التصوير
الإيجاد
القدر
التشابه
الآيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans are fully capable of creating their own actions without any divine involvement.
Correction: Maturidi argues that actions have two aspects: the creative aspect is solely from God, while the aspect of human intention and movement is from the human acquisition. If humans had full control over creation, evidence for God and prophets would be undermined.

Checkpoint

A) The world could be from an ignorant creator who does not know measures of things.
B) Miracles could be performed by humans without knowledge or power.
C) Both A and B.
D) Nothing would change.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that human actions are created by God (first aspect) and acquired by humans (second aspect), and that denying this leads to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you tend to take full credit for it, forgetting that your very intention and the ability to move are from God? How can you cultivate the awareness that your actions are a mix of divine gift and your choice? What will you do differently today to acknowledge God's role in your actions?
?
Do you ever feel that your life is entirely under your own control, leading to pride in success or despair in failure? How does understanding that the 'first aspect' of every action is from God change your reaction to successes and failures? Commit to one action today that reminds you of that first aspect.
?
The verse 'There is nothing like Him' underscores God's uniqueness. In what areas of your life do you tend to compare yourself to God, or think you can 'create' your own reality independent of Him? How can you internalize that your creativity is derivative, not original? What step can you take to align your sense of agency with this truth?

Good and Evil in Actions: From God, Not Inherent
Maturidi argues that good and evil in actions are not self-subsisting but from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the moral qualities of good and evil in human actions are not inherent to the actions themselves but are bestowed by God.

Core Idea
Good and evil in actions are not self-subsisting; they originate from God alone.

Maturidi begins by noting that human actions appear good or evil, yet the doers themselves are often unaware of their true moral standing. He then argues that these moral qualities cannot be intrinsic to the actions, because ignorance or knowledge alone does not change the moral status. Instead, the attribution of good and evil ultimately derives from God. In this passage, Maturidi anticipates and refutes the objection that actions are good or evil in themselves, showing that such a view would logically lead to the denial of God as Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the sequence: observation, argument, objection, refutation, conclusion.
The OCR has some unclear words (marked [?]), but the overall argument is clear.
2
Identify the key claim
Maturidi states that actions are not truly theirs (ليست لهم) in terms of moral quality. Find this line.
This is the central conclusion.
3
Find the objection and refutation
The objection is 'they are like that in themselves'. Maturidi refutes by showing it leads to absurdity: if good/evil need no originator, then everything could exist without a creator.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why does Maturidi emphasize that ignorance does not make an action evil nor knowledge make it good? How does this support his argument?
It shows that moral qualities are not dependent on human awareness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنا نجد أفعال العباد تخرج على حسن وقبح ، لا يعلم أهلها أنها تبلغ كونها على ذلك ، وهي في الحسن ذلك ولا في القبح ، بل هم عندهم نفسهم في تحسينها وتزيينها تخرج على غير ذلك ، بأن جعل أفعالهم على ما هي عليه ليست لهم ، ولو جاز لهم، وهم لا يعرفون مبلغ الحسن والقبح ، فإذا لا جهل يقبح الفعل ولا علم يحسنه ، فثبت أن فعلهم من هذا الوجه ليس لهم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . اللهم إلا أن يقولوا : هي لأنفسها كانت كذلك ، فإذا استقام حسن الفعل وقبحه لأتمرا له الفعل نفسه ، فالله تعالى به أحق من الشيء من نفسه ؛ إذ الشيء [؟] بحيث نفسه [؟] مع [؟] جاهل بما عليه . ما لو جاز كون حسن وقبح بلا منشى له الجاز كون كل شيء [بلا؟] وفي ذلك الخروج من الإسلام ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And also: We find the actions of servants emerge upon good and evil, while their doers do not know that they reach that state, and they are neither in that good nor in that evil. Rather, they themselves in their own estimation, in their making them good and adorning them, emerge other than that, because their actions are made as they are, not belonging to them. And if it were permissible for them, while they do not know the extent of good and evil, then ignorance does not make an action evil nor knowledge make it good. So it is established that their action from this aspect is not theirs, and there is no power except through Allah. However, unless they say: They were like that in themselves. Then if the good and evil of the action were established, the action itself would deserve its goodness or evilness. Then Allah, exalted, has more right to it than the thing itself, because the thing [؟] itself [؟] with [؟] ignorant of what it is upon. If it were permissible for good and evil to exist without an originator, then everything could exist [without?], and in that is departure from Islam, and there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 829

Commentary
This passage presents a proof regarding the source of good and evil in human actions. The author begins by observing that human actions appear as good or evil, yet the doers themselves are often unaware of the true extent or ultimate judgment of their actions. They assess and adorn their actions according to their own standards, but their actions are not truly theirs in terms of inherent moral quality. If it were argued that actions are self-subsisting in good or evil, then the action itself would deserve the attribution, but God is more deserving of that attribution. The objection is anticipated: someone might claim that actions have goodness or evil in themselves. The author refutes this by noting that if good and evil could exist without an originator (i.e., without God), then all things could exist without a creator, leading to a departure from Islam. Thus, the argument establishes that good and evil in actions derive from God, not from the actions themselves.

قبح
منشئ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that actions are inherently good or evil, independent of God.
Correction: Maturidi argues that moral qualities are not intrinsic; they originate from God. The alternative leads to unbelief.

Checkpoint

B) That humans determine good and evil by their own knowledge

D) That God is the only originator of actions

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that the moral qualities of actions are not self-subsisting but are dependent on God as the originator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Actions Cause Harm Yet Are Sought for Pleasure
Maturidi argues actions cause pain yet sought for pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that actions cannot be self-caused because they cause pain and fatigue, which the agent does not intend.

Core Idea
Actions are harmful, yet sought for pleasure—so they are not self-caused.

This passage continues Maturidi's refutation of the idea that actions are self-caused. He points to the observable fact that actions are tiring and painful, yet we do them for pleasure. This contradiction reveals that actions are not from the agent alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the key terms: مؤذية (harmful), متعبة (tiring), مؤلمة (painful).
The passage uses a logical argument structure.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
The premise: actions are harmful and tiring. The conclusion: actions are not self-caused. Identify the intermediate step about impossibility of harm without a cause.
3
Understand the logical force
Maturidi argues that since the agent intends pleasure but experiences harm, the harm must come from another cause—namely, God. This refutes self-causation.
The final phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' emphasizes dependence on God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنا نجد الأفعال مؤذية لأهلها ومتعبة ومؤلمة، ومحال تأذى الطبع بلا مؤذ وتعبه بلا متعب وتألمه بلا مؤلم ، فثبت أنها مؤلمة متعبة مؤذية إن قصد أربابها إلى أن يتلذذوا بها ويتمتعوا ، فثبت أنها كذلك لا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله بهم [؟]
And also: We find actions harmful to their doers, tiring and painful. And it is impossible for nature to be harmed without a harmer, tired without a cause of tiredness, and pained without a cause of pain. So it is established that they are painful, tiring, and harmful, even though their owners intend to delight and enjoy through them. So it is established that they are not [self-caused?], and there is no power except through Allah by them [؟].
Source pages p. 829

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument based on the observed painful and tiring nature of actions. Maturidi notes that actions are experienced as harmful and exhausting, yet the agents perform them with the intention of pleasure or benefit. It is impossible for harm to occur without a cause (a harmer, a tiring agent, a pain-giver). Since the actions themselves are the source of these effects, but the agents did not intend them (they intended pleasure), it follows that the actions are not self-caused; they are created by another, i.e., God. The final phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' reaffirms dependence on God.

Key Terms
مؤذية
متعبة
مؤلمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument denies natural causation, implying that actions have no real effects.
Correction: The argument does not deny that actions cause harm; it denies that the agent is the ultimate cause of that harm. The harm is real but its source is God, not the agent's intention.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of this argument regarding actions?
A) Actions are always pleasant
B) Actions are harmful and tiring, but intended for pleasure
C) Actions are self-caused
D) Nature itself is the cause of pain

Key Takeaway
Actions cause pain and fatigue, but agents seek pleasure, proving that actions are not self-caused but from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you notice that your own actions often bring you fatigue or pain even when you seek pleasure, do you pause to acknowledge that the outcome is not in your control? How does this realization challenge your sense of self-reliance? Today, commit to saying 'La quwwata illa billah' before beginning any task that requires effort, consciously handing the outcome to Allah.
?
Think of a specific action you performed recently that caused you harm or exhaustion, though you intended benefit. Did you, in that moment, attribute the harm to your own shortcomings or to Allah's decree? How would your heart’s state change if you firmly believed that Allah created both the intended pleasure and the unintended pain? This week, whenever you feel tired from a good deed, remind yourself: 'This tiredness is from Allah, and He will reward it.'
?
The argument shows that actions are not self-caused but from Allah. Is there an area in your life where you still behave as if your own power alone produces results—such as in your work, worship, or relationships? How would your daily routine change if you genuinely internalized this truth? Before sleeping tonight, identify one such area and make a dua asking Allah to make you truly reliant on Him.

No Creator but God: The Conventional Argument
Maturidi argues against human creation of actions using consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that attributing the origination of actions to humans would imply a second creator, contradicting the universal consensus that only God creates.

Core Idea
Only God is Creator—attributing creation to humans leads to polytheism.

Maturidi presents a powerful argument based on the universally accepted belief that only God is Creator. He shows that if we think humans bring their actions into existence, we would be giving them the attribute of creation, which contradicts this consensus. This passage is a key refutation of those who claim humans create their own actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: Maturidi starts with a consensus, then draws a hypothetical consequence.
The term 'المتعارف' refers to conventional knowledge accepted by all.
2
Identify the conventional belief Maturidi invokes
Find the phrase 'أن لا خالق غير الله ولا رب سواه'. This is the premise that all creation agrees upon.
3
Trace the logical implication
Maturidi shows that if actions emerge from humans, they would have the attribute of creation, implying another creator. This contradicts the premise, so the premise must be false.
The argument is: if humans create actions, then there is another creator; but consensus says no creator but God; therefore humans do not create actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا القول بالمتعارف في الخلق أن لا خالق غير الله ولا رب سواه ، ولو جعلنا حدث الأفعال وخروجها من العدم إلى الوجود ثم فناءها بعد الوجود ثم خروجها على تقدير من أربابها لجعلنا لها وصف الخلق الذي به صار الخلق خلقا ، وفي ذلك لزوم القول بخالق سواه ، وفي جوازه مناقضة قول من ذكرت . مع ما لو جاز ذلك الجاز القول برب فعله وذلك مدفوع ، وبالله التوفيق
And also: The conventional statement among creation is that there is no creator other than Allah and no lord besides Him. And if we were to make the origination of actions and their emergence from non-existence into existence, then their annihilation after existence, then their emergence upon a measure from their owners, we would have given them the attribute of creation by which creation becomes creation. And in that is the necessity of saying there is a creator other than Him, and in its permissibility is a contradiction of the statement of those mentioned. Moreover, if that were permissible, then the statement of a lord of the action would be permissible, and that is repudiated. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 829

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument based on the universally accepted belief that there is no creator but God. Maturidi argues that if we consider human actions as originating from non-existence and passing away, and then being produced again according to the agent's intention, we would be attributing the quality of 'creation' to those actions, which would imply a creator other than God. This contradicts the consensus (المتعارف) that only God is Creator. Moreover, if this were allowed, it would also allow the claim that humans are 'lords' of their actions, which is rejected. Therefore, human actions are not created by humans but by God.

Key Terms
المتعارف
خالق
رب
خلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans truly create their own actions in a real sense.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if humans create actions, then there would be another creator besides Allah, contradicting the consensus that only Allah creates. Therefore, humans do not create actions; actions are created by Allah.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, what would attributing the origination of actions to humans necessarily imply?
A) That humans are responsible for their actions
B) That there is another creator besides God
C) That actions are eternal
D) That God does not exist

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that only Allah creates, and attributing creation to humans contradicts universal consensus and leads to shirk.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Maturidi's argument shows that attributing creation to humans leads to shirk. Is there any 'creation' you claim for yourself — perhaps your career, relationships, or character — that you need to return to Allah? Will you make a specific intention today to see these as purely Allah's creation?

God's Power Over Human Actions
Maturidi argues for God's exclusive power over human actions, showing human power is temporary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that human power is temporary and granted by God, and that God is the ultimate creator of all actions.

Core Idea
Human power is a temporary gift; only God's power is eternal.

Imam Maturidi presents an argument based on the nature of human actions as movements and rests. He shows that the power we have is not independent but given by God for a fleeting moment. This lesson examines the logic behind the servant's dependency on God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully, noting the logical flow.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on حركات, سكون, قدرة. Understand how they relate.
3
Trace the argument
Follow Maturidi's reasoning: God is powerful over actions, He empowers the servant, the power leaves, so the servant is dependent.
4
Reflect on the implication
Understand that this affirms tawhid and negates any independent power in creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن العباد إذ أفعالهم في الحقيقة حركات وسكون في الظاهر ، والله قادر عليها ، لولا [ذلك] ما أقدرهم عليها ، فصارت هي لأنفسها تحت قدرته عليها ، فإذا أقدر العبد على ذلك ذهبت عنه القدرة فإذا قدرته زالت عنه ، وصار قادرا بقدرة تزول ، ومن ذلك وصفه فهو عبد لا رب ، والله الموفق . مع ما كانت الحركة والسكون ليسا بمخالفين في رأي العين لما كانا عليه ، ولا سبيل للناظر إلى التفريق بينهما ، ولولا حقيقة الاشتباه لاحتمل التفريق ، وفي تشابه الفعل لزوم القول فيها بما له وجبت التسمية في أحدهما ، وفي ذلك تشابه ؛ لأن استواء الأفعال في الشاهد يوجب تشابه الفاعلين ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And also: Indeed the servants, since their actions in reality are movements and rests in appearance, and Allah is powerful over them; if not for that, He would not have empowered them. So they became, in themselves, under His power. So when He empowers the servant over that, the power leaves him, and so when his power leaves him, he becomes powerful with a power that departs. And from that is his description: he is a servant, not a lord. And Allah is the Grantor of success. Moreover, movement and rest are not different in the eye sight from what they are, and there is no way for the observer to distinguish between them. Were it not for the reality of resemblance, distinction would be possible. And in the resemblance of action is the necessity of saying concerning them what necessitates the name in one of them. And in that is resemblance, because the equality of actions in the witnessed world necessitates the resemblance of the agents. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 829

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth argument focusing on the nature of human actions as movements and rests. Maturidi argues that God is powerful over all movements and rests; if He did not empower the servant, the servant could not perform them. The power granted to the servant is temporary and departs after the action, proving the servant is a servant, not a lord. Then an additional point: movements and rests are visually indistinguishable; they look alike. If they were from different agents, they would differ, but they are equal. This resemblance indicates that the same agent (God) creates them. If actions were from different agents, the actions would show differences. Therefore, all actions are created by God.

Key Terms
حركات
سكون
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans have independent power to act without God.
Correction: The argument shows that human power is granted by Allah and is temporary; without Allah's empowerment, no action can occur.

Checkpoint
What does the passage conclude about the servant based on the temporary nature of his power?
A) The servant is independent
B) The servant is a lord
C) The servant is a servant
D) The servant has eternal power

Key Takeaway
God alone has ultimate power; human power is a temporary gift, proving our servanthood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own actions today, do you truly feel that your power to do them was a temporary gift from Allah? Reflect on a moment you felt proud of your ability – can you now see it as from Him and then gone? How will you remind yourself of this dependency tomorrow?
?
This passage says the servant is not a lord. In what areas of your life do you act as if you are the lord, controlling outcomes? Identify one specific area where you must abandon that illusion and submit to Allah's sole power. What concrete step will you take?
?
The power to do good deeds is also temporary. Are you using this fleeting power to please Allah, or are you wasting it? Today, commit one action that acknowledges your power is from Him – like a moment of gratitude before you act.

Why Accidents Must Be from God Alone
Maturidi argues that the proof of the world's origination relies on accidents not attributable to human agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the proof of the world's origination depends on accidents being exclusively from God, and why attributing them to human actions would undermine the proof.

Core Idea
Accidents are God's exclusive signs; if humans could cause them, the proof of creation collapses.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a subtle but crucial argument: the very evidence we use to prove the world's temporality (accidents like movement and rest) would be invalidated if humans could produce them. He explains that if humans were true agents of these states, then the proof of the world's creation would be established by a creature, not by God, thereby nullifying the unique witness to God's oneness and lordship. This argument reinforces the Maturidi doctrine that human actions are not independent creators of their effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to get the overall argument. Maturidi uses a conditional structure to highlight the importance of attributing accidents to God.
The Arabic has some complexity; take your time to parse the conditional statements.
2
Analyze the conditional argument
Notice that Maturidi begins by stating the basis of the proof, then considers the counterfactual: if humans cause accidents, then the proof would be established by other than God. Follow the chain of consequences: the proof would be nullified, and bodies would not be perceived as God's making.
3
Reflect on the theological conclusion
Understand that the argument protects the uniqueness of God's proof. If humans were true agents, the evidence for God's existence would be invalidated. This reinforces that God alone creates accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الذي به عرف أهل التوحيد حدث الأعيان امتناعها عن الخروج من التفريق والاجتماع والتحرك والسكون ، فإذا لم يكن هذه الأحوال في الحقيقة الله على يدى من جرت عليه يديه لم نقدر أن نثبت جسم وعين يدرك على ما هو عليه بفعل الله ؛ إذ الأفعال التي ذكرنا من الأسماء يجوز تحقيقها لا بالله ، وإن كنا نبصر من به ذلك ، فيصير دليل حدث العالم يقيمه غير الله ؛ إذ لا سبيل له إلى إظهار الذي منه من الأحوال التي ذكرنا مما ليست منه ، ولولا تلك الأحوال لم يعرف حدث العالم فيبطل طريق العلم به بدليل أقامه هو ، ثم لما احتمل جميع الأحوال بغيره لم يثبت بها أنه صانع تلك ، والأجسام لا تعاين إلا بها ، فيبطل أن يكون الله تعالى جعل لوحدانيته دليلا يعرف ، ولربوبيته شاهدا يشهد على هذا القول ، وبالله العصمة والنجاة
And also: That by which the people of tawhid know the origination of bodies is their inability to emerge from separation, combination, movement, and rest. So if these states were not in reality from Allah, through the agency of him upon whose hands they occur, we could not establish a body or an eye perceived as it is by the act of Allah. Because the actions we mentioned from the names can be actualized without Allah, even though we see in whom it occurs. Then the proof of the world's origination would be established by other than Allah; for there is no way for him to manifest what is from the states we mentioned which are not from him. And were it not for those states, the world's origination would not be known, so the way of knowledge through a proof established by him would be nullified. Then, when he is capable of all states through other than him, it is not established by them that he is the maker of those states. And bodies are not perceived except through them. So it would be invalid that Allah, exalted, has made for His oneness a proof to be known, and for His lordship a witness to testify to this statement. And with Allah is protection and salvation.
Source pages p. 830

Commentary
This passage presents a fifth argument, which is more complex. Maturidi argues that the proof for the origination (temporality) of bodies is based on their inability to escape from accidents like separation, combination, movement, and rest. These accidents are the evidence that bodies are created. But if humans could produce these accidents themselves (through their own actions), then the proof would be established by a human agent, not by God. That would undermine the entire proof of the world's temporality because it would show that accidents can be produced by creatures. Moreover, if humans can produce these states, then the fact that bodies are subject to these states does not necessarily prove that God is their creator. Hence, the unique proof of God's oneness and lordship would be invalidated. The argument thus reinforces that only God creates these accidents, and human actions are not truly creative in this sense.

حدث
أعيان
تفريق
اجتماع
تحرك
سكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human actions are independent causes of accidents like movement and rest.
Correction: If humans were true agents of such accidents, the proof of the world's creation would be based on human action, not God, thereby invalidating the unique witness to God's oneness.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if humans could produce the accidents that indicate the world's temporality?
A) The world would be eternal
B) The proof of the world's origination would be established by someone other than God
C) Humans would become gods

Key Takeaway
The proof of the world's creation from accidents only holds if those accidents are caused solely by God, not by human agents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If you think you are, what does that mean for your belief in God's sole power of creation? Examine your conviction: do you live as if your actions are independent of Allah?
?
Maturidi says that if humans could produce these states, the proof of God's oneness would be invalid. That means every time you attribute your success, your failure, or even your next breath to your own effort alone, you are robbing Allah of His proof. How many times today have you forgotten that your very ability to act is a sign of Allah's exclusive creative power? The Quranic Argument for Divine Oneness from Unity of Creation
Maturidi cites Quran 23:91 to argue against multiple gods by linking the unity of creation to the oneness of the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the Quranic verse about multiple gods to prove that all accidents are created by one God.

Core Idea
If multiple gods existed, creation would be divided; but the unity of accidents proves one Creator.

Maturidi presents a Quranic argument that strikes at the heart of polytheism. By citing the verse that states each god would take his own creation, he shows that the interconnectedness of the material world demands a single Creator. This argument ties together the earlier discussions on accidents and divine unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its implication
Carefully read the Quranic verse quoted by Maturidi: 'Allah has not taken any son, nor was there any god with Him... then each god would have taken away what he created.' Consider what this implies about the relationship between multiple gods and creation.
The verse is from Surah Al-Mu'minun, verse 91.
2
Understand Maturidi's application to accidents
Notice that Maturidi says God has not created any accident without making it a proof. He links the verse to the accidents (a'rad) that he has been discussing. The verse suggests that if there were multiple gods, each would control his own creation, leading to chaos. The fact that accidents are coherently unified points to one Creator.
3
Reflect on the logical conclusion
Consider the argument: the unity and interconnectedness of the world (as seen in accidents) cannot be the product of multiple creators. The verse serves as a proof that all creation belongs to one God.
This argument is a concise Quranic proof for tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن الله تعالى قال : " ما اتخذ الله من ولد وما كان معه من إله " ثم قال : " إذا لذهب كل إله بما خلق " ثم الله جل ثناؤه لم يخلق عرضا قط إلا جعل عليه دليلا يعلم أنه خلق ؛ لما كانت الأعراض لما ذكرنا ، ويجوز أن يكون في خلقه خلق يجمع ويفرق ويحرك ويسكن ونحن لا نراه ، كما كان
And also: Allah, exalted, said: "Allah has not taken any son, nor was there any god with Him" then He said: "Then each god would have taken away what he created." Then Allah, mighty and majestic, has not created any accident except that He placed upon it a proof that it is created, because of the accidents as we mentioned. And it is possible that in His creation there is a creation that combines, separates, moves, and rests, and we do not see it, as it was.
Source pages p. 830

Commentary
This passage presents a sixth argument, citing Quran 23:91. Maturidi quotes the verse that states that if there were multiple gods, each would take his creation and they would be separate. Then he applies this to the discussion of accidents: God has not created any accident without making it a proof of His creation. The verse implies that all accidents (which are the basis for knowing God's oneness) are from one God. The argument ends with a thought that there might be unseen creations that also combine and separate, but we do not see them. The passage is cut off, but the main point is that the unity of creation (all accidents being created by God) proves His oneness.

Key Terms
ولد
إله
خلق
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse only talks about God not having a son, not about multiple gods or creation.
Correction: Maturidi uses the verse analogically: the phrase 'nor was there any god with Him' and the conditional 'each god would have taken away what he created' directly address the issue of multiple creators. The verse is a proof for tawhid in both sonship and partnership.

Checkpoint
How does the verse 'If there were other gods, each would take away what he created' support Maturidi's argument?
A) It shows that God has a son
B) It proves that all creations belong to one God, otherwise they would be separate
C) It denies the existence of accidents
D) It says humans cannot create

Key Takeaway
The Quranic argument from the unity of creation demonstrates that all accidents, and thus the entire cosmos, are the work of a single Creator without partner.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the order and unity of creation—from the consistency of natural laws to the harmony of your own life—do you genuinely see it as a sign that there is only one Creator, or do you sometimes take it for granted? How would your worship change if you truly internalized that every accident, every event, is from Him alone?
?
The verse says that if there were multiple gods, each would take what he created. Are there areas in your life where you act as if something else has independent control—your career, your health, your relationships—as if they are 'gods' that can take away what they create? What would it mean for you to surrender that illusion today?
?
Maturidi uses the unity of accidents as a proof. Think about the 'accidents' in your own life—sudden joys, misfortunes, changes. Do you see them all as coming from one Hand, or do you divide them between Allah and other causes? How can you train your heart to see every single accident as a direct proof of His oneness?

Divine Decree
intermediate
5 min
Divine Empowerment Entails Divine Creation
Maturidi argues that divine empowerment logically entails divine creation via analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that divine enabling (iqdar) logically implies Allah's power over the act and His creation of it.

Core Idea
If Allah empowers, He must have power over the act alone.

Imam Al-Maturidi presents a concise logical argument linking God's empowerment of the servant to His power and creation of the act. Using an illustrative analogy with teaching, he shows that the enabler must possess both knowledge and power over what he enables. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical connectors.
Maturidi's Arabic is concise and sequential.
2
Understand the analogy with teaching
Maturidi draws a parallel between enabling someone to act and teaching someone knowledge. Just as a teacher must know the subject, an enabler must have power over the act.
This analogy (tamthil) is central to the argument.
3
Trace the inference from enabling to creation
Once it is established that Allah has power over the act, Maturidi argues that what Allah has power over cannot exist from another. Therefore, the act is created by Allah.
This step moves from divine power to exclusive creation.
4
Reflect on the implication for human power
Consider how this argument preserves human agency (empowerment) while affirming that Allah alone creates the act. This refutes the view that humans create their own acts.
Maturidi’s position distinguishes between acquisition (kasb) and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن العبد يقدر بإقدار الله إياه ، فلا يجوز أن يقدر بإقدار من ليست لا علم له به . أو لا يرى أنه له القدرة عليه ، كما لا يجوز أن يعلم بإعلام من لا علم له به . ولا يجوز لأحد القدرة على إقدار غيره على شيء لم يقدر هو عليه ، ومن علم يعلم به غيره لم يجز أن لا يعلم هو ، فمثله الذي بينا ، واذا ثبتت قدرة الله عليه ، وما يقدر / الله عليه فهو محال وجوده بغيره ثبت أنه خالق ذلك
And also that the servant is empowered by Allah's enabling him, so it is not permissible that he be empowered by an enabler who has no knowledge of it, or does not see that he has power over it, just as it is not permissible that one be taught by a teacher who has no knowledge. And it is not permissible for anyone to have the power to enable another over something over which he himself lacks power. And whoever has knowledge, another knows by it, it is not permissible that he himself does not know. So the analogy is what we have set forth. And if Allah's power over it is established, and what Allah has power over is impossible for anything else to exist, it is established that He is the Creator of that.
Source pages p. 831

Commentary
Maturidi uses the principle that an effect cannot exceed its cause. The servant's power to act comes from Allah's enabling (iqdar). The enabler must have knowledge of the act and power over it; otherwise, it would be like being taught by an ignorant teacher. If the enabler does not have power over the act, he cannot enable another to do it. Therefore, since Allah enables the servant, Allah must have power over the act. And that which Allah has power over cannot possibly exist from another (since Allah's power is exclusive). Hence, the act must be created by Allah. This analogical argument (tamthil) links enabling to power to creation.

Key Terms
إقدار (iqdar)
قدرة (qudra)
خلق (khalq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this negates human choice; Maturidi distinguishes power as enabling, not compulsion.
Correction: Maturidi does not deny human responsibility; he affirms that Allah empowers the servant, but the power (qudra) to act is given by Allah. The act is created by Allah, while the servant acquires (kasb) the act. This preserves both divine omnipotence and human accountability.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what must be true of the one who empowers another to do something?
A) They must have knowledge and power over that thing
B) They must be physically present
C) They must be greater in rank
D) They must have created the person

Key Takeaway
Divine enabling (iqdar) logically implies that Allah has power over the act and is its Creator, preserving both divine omnipotence and human acquisition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to act by your own power, do you truly acknowledge that Allah's enabling (iqdar) is the real source of every action you take, or do you feel independent? How can you train yourself to feel a constant sense of reliance on Him?
?
If Allah’s power over your actions is absolute, why do you still feel anxious about outcomes? Are you trusting in your own competence or in Allah's creation of the act? This week, pause before each major action and say, 'Allah, You have enabled this, and You are its Creator.'
?
The argument uses an analogy: just as a teacher must know what he teaches, so Allah must have power over what He enables. Have you ever sought empowerment from someone else—like a leader, friend, or your own ability—while ignoring that true power belongs only to Allah? How can you shift your reliance to Him alone starting today?

Divine Decree
intermediate
6 min
Refuting the Objection: Does Creation Entail Self-Command?
Maturidi refutes the objection that if God creates actions, He commands Himself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the objection that divine creation of human actions would entail God commanding and prohibiting Himself.

Core Idea
Commanding an action is not the same as commanding its creation.

This passage presents a key objection against the view that God creates all human actions, and then provides a sharp refutation. The objector claims that if God creates, then He commands Himself. Maturidi's answer exposes a confusion between commanding the act and commanding its creation. Let's read how he dismantles this objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part where the objector argues: if God creates actions, then commanding and prohibiting those actions would be commanding and prohibiting Himself. Understand the logical structure.
The objection assumes a direct link between creating and commanding.
2
Identify the refutation's strategy
Maturidi asks a dichotomous question: does the command to the servant imply a command to create? He will then refute both possible answers.
This is a classic dialectical move.
3
Trace the consequences of each answer
If the objector says 'yes,' then God commands people to be creators, which is unanimously rejected. If 'no,' then the objection's assumption is false. Understand the implications of each.
4
Grasp the key distinction
The command is to perform the act, not to create it. Creation is exclusive to God. This distinction resolves the paradox.
This is the core insight of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاحتج من يأبى القول به في خلق الأفعال: أول شيء أنهم أمروا بها ونهوا عنها، وذكروا الآيات في الأمر بها والنهي، ولو جعلناها خلقا له لكان أمر نفسه ونهى عن خلق ذلك. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: فيقال لمن احتج به: أتقول أمر العبد بخلق الإيمان ونحوه، ونهى عن خلق الكفر ونحوه؟ فإن قال: بلى، صرح بأن الله تعالى أمر الناس أن يكونوا خالقين، وقد أبى المسلمون أن يكون غيره خالقا. ولم يختلف المسلمون في جواز عبادة الخالق مطلقا، وأن الخالق هو الرب وهو الإله، فيجب بهذا جعل كل عبد كذلك، وذلك مما أباه الجميع. وإن قال: لا، قيل: فإذ لم يوجب الأمر بالفعل والنهي عنه أمرا بالخلق ونهيا عنه، لم قلت إنه لو كان الله خالق ذلك يوجب الأمر له والنهي عنه، ولم يثبت من الوجه الذي فيه الأمر والنهي أمرا بالخلق وغيره؟
The one who refrains from saying that [God creates] actions argued: First, they [humans] are commanded and prohibited regarding them, and they mentioned the verses about commanding and prohibiting. If we made those actions a creation of His, then He would be commanding Himself and prohibiting Himself from creating that. Al-Faqih (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is said to the one who argued thus: Do you say that God commands the servant to create faith and the like, and prohibits creating disbelief and the like? If he says: Yes, then he declares that God commanded people to be creators, and the Muslims have refused that anyone other than Him be a creator. And the Muslims have not disagreed about the permissibility of worshiping the Creator absolutely, and that the Creator is the Lord and the God. So by this, it would be necessary to make every servant like that, and that which the whole community refuted. If he says: No, then it is said: Since the command to the act and the prohibition of it do not necessitate a command to create or a prohibition of it, why did you say that if God were the Creator of that, it would necessitate a command to Him and a prohibition from Him? And it has not been established from the aspect of command and prohibition a command to create or other than that.
Source pages p. 835

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from those who deny that God creates human actions, and then provides a refutation. The objector argues: If God created human actions, then since humans are commanded and prohibited regarding those actions, it would mean God is commanding and prohibiting Himself, because He is the creator of those actions. The answer from al-Faqih is to ask the objector: Do you claim that the command to the servant is a command to create faith or disbelief? If the objector says yes, then he would be claiming that God commands people to be creators, which is unanimously rejected by Muslims because creation belongs only to God. If the objector says no, then the command to the act is not a command to create. So why would the fact that God creates actions necessitate that He is commanding Himself? The command and prohibition are not about the act of creation but about the act itself. Therefore, the objection fails.

Key Terms
خلق الأفعال
الأمر والنهي
الخالق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God creates human actions, then commanding those actions would mean God commands Himself.
Correction: The command to perform an act is separate from the act's creation. God's command addresses the servant, not His own creative act.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's answer, what does the command to believe entail?
A) A command to create belief
B) A command to perform the act of believing
C) A command to God to create belief
D) A command to angels

Key Takeaway
Commanding an action does not imply commanding its creation; thus the objection that God would command Himself is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever feel a whisper that 'God created this action, so why is He angry with me?' This lesson destroys that whisper. Take a moment now: What sin have you been excusing with this thought? Repent from that excuse and affirm that you own the act even though God creates it.
?
The objector tried to make God responsible for His own commands. In your heart, have you ever felt resentment toward God's commandments as if they were burdensome impositions? This lesson shows that God's command is not a command to yourself but a gift to guide you. What command have you been resenting? Will you embrace it today as a mercy?
?
This lesson demands that you distinguish between God's creative power and your moral responsibility. Are you ready to accept full responsibility for your choices without blaming your circumstances or even God's decree? Name one area of your life where you have been avoiding this responsibility and commit to changing your attitude starting now.

Divine Decree
intermediate
5 min
Faith and Disbelief as Comprehensive Signs
Maturidi deconstructs faith and disbelief as signs and questions the scope of command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that faith and disbelief are not simple acts but complex signs of a person's wisdom, folly, knowledge, and ignorance, and why command and prohibition cannot apply to these indicative aspects.

Core Idea
Faith and disbelief are signs of human character, not bare acts subject to command.

Imam Maturidi continues his argument by examining the nature of faith and disbelief. He asks his opponent to consider whether these realities are ever without being accidents, movements, proofs of wisdom or foolishness, and manifestors of knowledge or ignorance. The opponent must concede that they always carry these aspects. This sets up the crucial question: does the command or prohibition regarding an act also apply to these aspects? Maturidi shows that it cannot, leading to an important concession.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the descriptions
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how Maturidi describes faith and disbelief: as two things, two accidents, two movements, two proofs, and two manifestors. Each pair emphasizes a different function of these concepts.
The phrase 'لا بد من بلى' forces agreement that faith/disbelief always have these aspects.
2
Understand the question about command and prohibition
Maturidi then asks: does the command/prohibition regarding the act also command/prohibit these aspects (like being a sign of foolishness)? If the opponent says yes, Maturidi shows it leads to absurdity.
The key point: disbelief as evidence of foolishness is true (صدق), and prohibiting what is true is impossible.
3
Recognize the opponent's forced concession
Since many people do not even know these attributes, command and prohibition cannot apply universally. Hence, the opponent must concede that command/prohibition are not about these aspects.
This concession paves the way for Maturidi's conclusion in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له: حدثنا عن الإيمان والكفر، هل يخلوان من أن يكونا شيئين عرضين وحركتين دليلين على حدث الفاعل، وحجتين على حكمة الرجل وسفهه، ومظهرى علمه وجهله؟ لا بد من بلى؛ لما فيها هذه الوجوه كلها. فيقال: هل الأمر والنهي بالفعل موجبا الأمر والنهي بهذه الوجوه التي في فعله ذلك؟ فإن قال: نعم، أحال؛ لما في كفره دليل سفهه، وهو من حيث الدلالة صدق، ومحال النهي عنه من ذلك الوجه، ولأن كثيرا منهم لا يعرفون تلك الصفات له لم يجز الأمر لذلك ذلك الوجه ولا النهي، فلا بد من المساعدة لهم في ذلك، فيقال له: ما منع أن يكون ذلك خلقا، وليس في ذلك أمر لنفسه بالخلق ولا نهي؟ ثم استقام في العقل الجهات التي بينا، مع ما أوصاف الإضافات أن ذا أصغر من ذا وأكبر، وأخير وأشر، وأقبح وأحسن من ذلك وأعظم في الحجة وأوضع وأضعف وأقوى، وأنه حدث وموجود، وغير ذلك مما يكثر وصفه، ولا يوصف شيء من ذلك بالشر والخير من جميع الوجوه ولا بالطاعة والمعصية، فجائز خلقها، ولا يوصف من ذلك الوجه بطاعة ولا معصية، ولا خير ولا شر، ولا أمر ولا نهى، ولا شيء مما له الفعل، والله الموفق.
Then it is said to him: Inform us about faith and disbelief, do they ever lack being two accidental things and two movements that indicate the temporality of the agent, and two proofs for the wisdom of a man and his foolishness, and manifestors of his knowledge and ignorance? It must be yes; because all these aspects are in them. So it is said: Does the command and prohibition regarding the act necessitate command and prohibition of these aspects that are in that act? If he says: Yes, then it is impossible; for in his disbelief there is evidence of his foolishness, and it is true as an indication, and it is impossible to prohibit it from that aspect. And because many of them do not know those attributes of him, it is not permissible to command or prohibit from that aspect. So it is necessary to concede to them in that. Then it is said to him: What prevents that [act] from being created, while there is no command to Himself to create nor prohibition? Then the aspects we mentioned are intellectually sound, along with the attributes of relations that this is smaller than that and larger, better and worse, uglier and more beautiful than that, greater in proof and clearer, weaker and stronger, and that it is temporal and existent, and other things that are frequently described. And nothing of that is described as evil or good from all aspects, nor as obedience or disobedience. So it is permissible for Him to create them, and it is not described from that aspect as obedience or disobedience, nor good nor evil, nor command nor prohibition, nor anything of what pertains to the act. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 835

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument. The opponent is asked to consider faith and disbelief: they are accidents and movements that indicate the temporality of the agent (the human), and they are proofs of wisdom or foolishness. The question is whether the command and prohibition regarding the act also command and prohibit these aspects (like being a sign of foolishness). If the opponent says yes, then it would be impossible because disbelief is evidence of foolishness, and it is true as an indication, so it cannot be prohibited from that aspect. Many people do not even know those attributes. Therefore, the command and prohibition are not about these aspects. So why would creating the action entail commanding or prohibiting those aspects? The conclusion is that it is permissible for God to create actions, and they are not described as obedience or disobedience from that creative aspect. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الكفر
العرض
الحركة
الدليل
الحجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that faith and disbelief are simply acts of obedience or disobedience, devoid of deeper indicative significance.
Correction: Maturidi shows they are inherently signs of wisdom/folly and knowledge/ignorance, which are not subject to command or prohibition.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say it is impossible for command and prohibition to apply to the aspect of disbelief being evidence of foolishness?
A) Because disbelief is not created by God.
B) Because the evidence of foolishness is true, and it would be absurd to prohibit what is true.
C) Because command and prohibition only apply to the act itself, not its attributes.
D) Because the command is only for believers.

Key Takeaway
Faith and disbelief carry inherent indicative qualities that are not subject to command or prohibition, forcing a concession from the opponent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own faith, do you see it merely as an act you perform, or as a sign of what is truly inside you? If your faith were stripped of its outward actions, what would your inner state reveal? Are you satisfied with that sign?
?
Maturidi argues that the indicative nature of disbelief (its truth as evidence of folly) cannot be subject to command. How does this change the way you view your own moments of disbelief or doubt? Are they just failures to obey, or do they reveal something deeper about your soul?
?
The passage says that many people do not recognize the attributes of wisdom or folly in others, so command and prohibition do not apply to those aspects. But you know your own inner state. What aspect of your faith or disbelief have you been ignoring because no one commands or forbids it? Will you continue to ignore it, or will you let this lesson push you to examine it?

Divine Decree

The Permissibility of Divine Creation
Maturidi concludes that divine creation of actions is permissible and morally neutral in itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's conclusion that God's creation of actions is permissible and cannot be described as obedience or disobedience from the aspect of creation.

Core Idea
God's creation of actions is neutral; moral labels apply only to human acquisition.

Having secured the concession that command and prohibition do not cover the indicative aspects of actions, Maturidi now draws the full conclusion. He asks directly: what prevents the act from being created by God? There is no command or prohibition from God regarding His own creative act. Moreover, attributes like 'better' or 'worse' only apply relationally, not absolutely. Hence, creation is permissible, and from that aspect, no moral or legal description attaches.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding argument
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the question 'ما منع أن يكون ذلك خلقا' and the list of relational attributes.
Notice the repetition of 'ولا يوصف' to emphasize neutrality.
2
Understand the list of relational attributes
Maturidi mentions attributes like smaller/bigger, better/worse, etc., which are relative and not absolute moral categories. They do not entail obedience or disobedience.
These examples show that many descriptions are neutral in terms of moral command.
3
Grasp the final conclusion
The conclusion is clear: it is permissible for God to create actions, and from the aspect of creation they are not described as obedience, disobedience, good, evil, command, or prohibition.
This defends the Ash'ari/Maturidi position of kasb (acquisition) against the Mu'tazilite denial of divine creation of actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It must be yes; because all these aspects are in them. So it is said: Does the command and prohibition regarding the act necessitate command and prohibition of these aspects that are in that act? And nothing of that is described as evil or good from all aspects, nor as obedience or disobedience. So it is permissible for Him to create them, and it is not described from that aspect as obedience or disobedience, nor good nor evil, nor command nor prohibition, nor anything of what pertains to the act. And Allah is the Grantor of success.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if God creates an action, He is thereby commanding or approving it, or that creation itself carries moral value.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that God's creative act is distinct from moral prescription; creation is neutral and does not imply command or prohibition.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Maturidi gives for the permissibility of God creating actions?
A) Because God has complete power over everything.

C) Because humans cannot create anything themselves.
D) Because actions are not real entities.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own sins, do you ever subtly blame God for creating that action in you, making you feel less responsible? How does this lesson force you to own your sins completely, since creation itself carries no moral label? Commit today to repent without excuses.
?
If relational attributes like better/worse are not absolute good or evil, in what areas of your life have you been treating relative matters as moral absolutes? How can you realign your judgments with the distinction this lesson makes?
?
God's act of creating your actions is neutral; only your acquisition gives them moral weight. Are you living as if that is true—taking full responsibility for your choices? Or do you still attribute your failings to divine decree? What one change will you make starting today?

Principle of Threat and Promise and Burden of Proof
Extends reasoning to threat/promise; states burden of proof principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's extension of command/prohibition to divine reward/punishment and the burden of proof on those who deny God's creation of actions.

Core Idea
Denying God's creation of actions requires proof, not just intuition.

Maturidi continues his argument by applying the same reasoning to divine threat and promise. He then lays down a crucial methodological principle: those who deny that God creates human actions cannot rely on mere repugnance but must provide evidence. This passage sharpens the debate between those who uphold divine omnipotence and those who see human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Maturidi uses short, parallel phrases.
2
Identify the three reasons for denial
The passage lists three possible grounds for denying that God creates actions. Find each one: impossibility, lack of evidence, and repugnance/necessitation. Understand how they differ.
The third reason combines two effects: necessitation and repugnance.
3
Understand the burden of proof
Maturidi states that the one who denies on grounds of impossibility must bring proof. Consider why this shifts the burden from the affirmative to the negative claim.
This is a key methodological move in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى مثل ذلك أمر الوعيد والوعد، إنا حققنا الفعل، فلزم فيه الأمر والنهي، فمثله يلزم الثواب والعقاب. ثم الأصل في هذا أن يكون القول بخلق الأفعال إما أن ينكر للإحالة أو لما لا دلالة على القول بذلك أو لما في القول به في إيجاب الضرورة وارتفاع الإمكان، ويقبح في العقول الأمر والنهي والوعد والوعيد، فما كان هذا سبيله، فمن أبى القول به للإحالة كلف دليله على ذلك، ولن يجد إلا على
And similarly is the matter of threat and promise. Indeed we have established the action, so command and prohibition become necessary regarding it; similarly reward and punishment become necessary. Then the principle in this is that the statement about the creation of actions is either denied due to impossibility, or due to lack of evidence for that statement, or because the statement entails necessitation and removal of possibility, and that command, prohibition, promise, and threat become repugnant in the intellects. If that is the case, then whoever denies it due to impossibility is required to provide evidence for that, and he will not find except [incomplete].
Source pages p. 835

Commentary
This passage extends the previous reasoning to divine threat and promise. Just as command and prohibition follow from the fact that the action is real (created by God?), so too reward and punishment follow. Then Maturidi states a methodological principle: the denial of God's creation of actions can only be based on logical impossibility, or lack of evidence, or because it entails necessity and removes possibility, or because it makes command, prohibition, promise and threat repugnant to reason. The burden of proof is on the denier if he claims impossibility, and he will never find evidence. The text cuts off, but the argument is clear: those who deny creation of actions on grounds of impossibility must provide a proof, which they cannot.

Misconception: The repugnance of command and prohibition is sufficient reason to deny God's creation of actions.
Correction: Maturidi argues that repugnance alone is not enough; if one denies due to impossibility, proof must be provided.

C) They must reject all proofs.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that denial of God's creation of actions must be based on proof of impossibility, not mere repugnance.

The Multiple Aspects of Actions and Divine Creation
Maturidi argues that command and prohibition do not apply to all aspects of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that command and prohibition do not apply to all aspects of actions, thus permitting divine creation of actions without contradiction.

Core Idea
Actions have aspects; command applies only to human acquisition, not divine creation.

Maturidi engages an opponent who argues that if God creates human actions, commanding or forbidding them is meaningless. He responds by analyzing the multiple aspects present in any action—such as faith or disbelief—and showing that command and prohibition only pertain to certain aspects. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and establishes the rational basis for divine creation of actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: a question, an objection, an answer, and a generalization.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key problem
The opponent claims that if God creates actions, command/prohibition are invalid. Maturidi begins by analyzing faith and disbelief—do they have multiple aspects?
Faith and disbelief are examples of actions with multiple aspects.
3
Follow the argument's structure
Trace how Maturidi shows that not all aspects can be commanded or forbidden. He points out that forbidding evidence of folly (in disbelief) would be impossible, and that many people are unaware of these attributes.
This leads to the conclusion that command/prohibition do not cover all aspects.
4
Understand the permissibility of creation
Maturidi then asks: what prevents the action from being created by God, without implying command or prohibition on God? He gives examples of relational attributes that are not judged as good/evil.
Creation of these aspects is therefore permissible.
5
Reflect on the general principle
Finally, Maturidi outlines why one might deny creation of actions: impossibility, lack of evidence, or entailment of necessity. He challenges opponents to provide proof.
The passage cuts off, but the principle is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له : حدثنا عن الإيمان والكفر ، هل يخلوان من أن يكونا شيئين عرضين وحركتين دليلين على حدث الفاعل ، وحجتين على حكمة الرجل وسفهه ، ومظهرى علمه وجهله ؟ لا بد من بلى ؛ لما فيها هذه الوجوه كلها . فيقال : هل الأمر والنهى بالفعل موجبا الأمر والنهى بهذه الوجوه التى فى فعله ذلك ؟ فان قال : نعم : ، أحال ؛ لما في كفره دليل سفهه ، وهو من حيث الدلالة صدق ، ومحال النهى عنه من ذلك الوجه ، ولأن كثيرا منهم لا يعرفون تلك الصفات له لم يجز الأمر لذلك ذلك الوجه ولا النهى ، فلا بد من المساعدة لهم في ذلك ، فيقال له : ما منع أن يكون ذلك خلق ، وليس فى ذلك أمر لنفسه بالخلق ولا نهى ؟ ثم استقام في العقل الجهات التى بينا ، مع ما أوصاف الإضافات أن ذا أصغر من ذا وأكبر ، و[أخير وأشر؟]، وأقبح وأحسن من ذلك وأعظم في الحجة و[أوضع؟] وأضعف وأقوى ، وأنه حدث وموجود ، وغير ذلك مما يكثر وصفه ، ولا يوصف شيء من ذلك بالشر والخير من جميع الوجوه ولا بالطاعة والمعصية ، فجائز خلقها ، ولا يوصف من ذلك الوجه بطاعة ولا معصية ، ولا خير ولا شر ، ولا أمر ولا نهى ، ولا شيء مما له الفعل ، والله الموفق . وعلى مثل ذلك أمر الوعيد والوعد ، إنا حققنا الفعل ، فلزم فيه الأمر والنهي، فمثله يلزم الثواب والعقاب . ثم الأصل فى هذا أن يكون القول بخلق الأفعال إما أن ينكر للإحالة أو لما لا دلالة على القول بذلك أو لما في القول به في ايجاب الضرورة وارتفاع الإمكان ، ويقبح فى العقول الأمر والنهي والوعد والوعيد فما كان هذا سبيله ، فمن أبى القول به للإحالة كلف دليله على ذلك ، ولن يجد إلا على
Then it is said to him: Inform us about faith and disbelief: are they free from being two accidents and two movements that are evidences of the temporality of the agent, and two proofs of the man's wisdom and folly, and two manifestors of his knowledge and ignorance? There is no avoiding 'yes'; because all these aspects are present in them. So it is said: Does the command and prohibition of the action necessitate command and prohibition of these aspects that are in that action? If he says: Yes, he makes an impossibility; because in his disbelief there is evidence of his folly, and from the aspect of evidence it is true, and prohibition of it from that aspect is impossible, and because many of them do not know those attributes for him, so command and prohibition from that aspect are not permissible. So there is no avoiding helping them in that. So it is said to him: What prevents that action from being creation, while there is in that no command for itself to create nor prohibition? Then the aspects we mentioned become rationally sound, along with the attributes of relations: that this is smaller than that and larger, and [better and worse?], and uglier and more beautiful than that, and greater in argument and [clearer?] and weaker and stronger, and that it is temporal and existent, and other things whose description is abundant. Nothing of that is described as evil or good from all aspects, nor as obedience or disobedience. So it is permissible to create them, and they are not described from that aspect as obedience or disobedience, nor good nor evil, nor command nor prohibition, nor anything that has action. And Allah is the Grantor of success. And upon the like of that is the matter of threat and promise: we have established the action, so command and prohibition are entailed, and similarly reward and punishment are entailed. Then the origin in this is that the statement about the creation of actions is either denied due to impossibility, or due to lack of evidence for the statement, or due to the statement entailing necessity and removal of possibility and making command, prohibition, promise and threat ugly in the intellects. So whatever is on this path, whoever refuses the statement due to impossibility is asked for proof thereof, and he will not find [it] except...
Source pages p. 836

Commentary
This passage is an argument defending the possibility of God's creation of human actions without contradicting command and prohibition. Maturidi begins by asking whether faith and disbelief are not composed of multiple aspects: accidents, movements, evidence of temporality, wisdom/folly, knowledge/ignorance. Since all these aspects are present, he asks if commanding or forbidding the action also commands or forbids these aspects. If the opponent says yes, a contradiction arises: in disbelief there is evidence of folly, which is true, and forbidding truth is impossible. Also many do not know those attributes. Thus the opponent must concede that not all aspects are subject to command. Maturidi then asks what prevents that action from being created by God, without implying God commands or prohibits Himself. He notes that the aspects mentioned are rationally sound, and attributes like larger/smaller, better/worse, etc. are not judged as good/evil or obedience/disobedience from all aspects. So creation of these aspects is permissible. He extends this to divine promise and threat: since action is established, command/prohibition follow, and similarly reward/punishment. Finally he states the general principle: the claim of creation of actions is denied either due to impossibility, lack of evidence, or entailment of necessity removing possibility. Whoever denies it due to impossibility must provide proof, but will not find it (sentence cut off).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates human actions, commanding or forbidding them is meaningless because God would be commanding Himself.
Correction: Maturidi shows that command and prohibition apply only to aspects related to the servant's acquisition, not to the aspect of divine creation. Many aspects of an action are neutral and thus can be created by God without contradiction.

Checkpoint

B) Because actions like faith and disbelief have multiple aspects not all subject to command.
C) Because God does not create human actions.
D) Because command and prohibition are only for actions, not creations.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that actions have multiple aspects, and command/prohibition apply only to the aspect of human acquisition, not to the aspect of divine creation, thus making divine creation of actions rationally permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever whisper to yourself that 'Allah created it, so I am not to blame'? In light of this lesson, how will you now confront the aspect of your own acquisition and the command that applies to you? Refuting the Objection of Two Agents with Physical Examples
Maturidi provides physical examples to show one effect from two agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how everyday examples of two agents producing a single effect demonstrate that divine creation and human acquisition can coexist.

Core Idea
Two agents can produce one effect, as shown by ordinary physical actions.

Maturidi begins by countering the claim that an action cannot have two real agents. He uses tangible examples from human experience—like two people dragging a breaking stone—to show that multiple agents can indeed produce a single outcome. This sets the foundation for reconciling God's creative role with human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the literal meaning.
Notice the examples are drawn from everyday physical actions.
2
Identify the examples
Identify the specific examples Maturidi gives (two people dragging, removing, carrying an atom). Ask: how do these show one effect from two agents?
3
Connect to theological context
Relate the examples back to the theological debate: God’s creation and human acquisition. Understand that the examples support the compatibility of divine and human agency.
The key distinction is between 'action' (divine) and 'effect' (human).

Source Text
